Abstract
Title of the paper
Platform Design for Sharing Novel Driver Perspective Video as a Remedy in Fault
Claims - Validating the business case
Keywords
Insurance data; Traﬃc accidents; Data protection; Data interaction; Interface
design; Web application; Usability; Driver perspective video; Insurance tool; Share
video; Privacy;
Abstract
The purpose of this project is to validate a start-up business case where the use of
driver perspective video is used as evidence in insurance cases, i.e., video recorded
through a pair of glasses worn by the driver. Current evidence materials are either
not available to the insurance company, or the evidence does not support the driver
as intended, thereby prolonging the case, which often results in a loss. As such, an
online prototype platform for sharing the recorded driver perspective video is created
through human-centered development where interviews with haulers, drivers, and
insurance agents have shaped the platform.
Initial observations suggested that drivers could be opposed to the role of the data
subject on the platform. It was hypothesized that lack of information in regards
to the video was the cause of reluctance. An experiment showed that drivers who
are familiar with the platform, i.e., drivers who have seen the video content are
more likely to accept being the data subject. A paired t-test of the diﬀerence in
the before and after video exposure provided a value of 0.05 > p > 0.025. With
a p-value less than 0.05, there is strong evidence that the exposure does have an
impact. In the after scenario, 6/6 participants approved the video for sharing with
hauler and insurance companies respectively, and 4/6 approved the video to be used
by researchers.
In conclusion, the business case is validated based interviews with two haulers
and further validated through usability tests with four insurance agents. However,
the low amount of participants is considered an overall constraint on the project.
Furthermore, it is recommended to expand the user segment and provide access for
drivers.

Preface
This thesis was conducted at the Technical University of Denmark at the
Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science where two study lines
were joined to fulﬁll the project requirements speciﬁed by the partnering company.
As such, this thesis represents the fulﬁllment of the requirements for acquiring a
Cand.Polyt degree in Computer Science and Digital Media Engineering for the
respective authors.
The current project is based upon the architecture presented in the preceding work
by Persson and Thomsen in the report: Product development and user experience
validation of augmented reality in mitigating blind spots[1]. The project is concluded
with equal contribution, where the focus area of Otterstrøm has been security and
the focus area of Hansen has been user experience.
Authors

M.Sc. Software Engineering
Technical University of Denmark

Introduction 1
With new technology comes new data. That is an abbreviated version of the pitch
initiating this thesis; A pitch given by the start-up company, AUGIT Solutions.
Behind the company is a grand vision of aiding truck drivers and haulers, and a
goal to eliminate the distressing blind spot problem. This is achieved by the use
of Augmented Reality (AR) where the driver is presented with the sensation of the
truck being transparent through a pair of AR glasses. AR is a fairly new technology
making its way into the domain of transport where new-comers such as AUGIT
and old-timers such as the car manufacturer Nissan are currently in the process of
deﬁning diﬀerent use-cases for the technology. As stated, the AUGIT vision is to
eliminate blind spots by utilizing the natural vision of the drivers. This enables
them to look directly where they want to see instead of consulting various mirrors
to properly orient themselves in their surroundings.
Although still in an early prototype phase, the solution has caught the interest of
multiple stakeholders and was recently showcased at the largest transport exhibition
in Scandinavia, Transport Herning 2019. As the solution is currently being matured
through user tests, the possibilities for the potential data in the form of video footage
is considered with great curiosity. Video footage in traﬃc is not a new concept,
however, having video from the drivers perspective is a novelty. Such videos can
be recorded through the AUGIT AR glasses which are worn by the driver, hence
the footage will follow the head movements of the driver and be a representation of
what is within the driver’s respective ﬁeld of view.
One interest in the new footage emanates from the insurance company CODAN
who, in cooperation with AUGIT, hypothesized the video being used as evidence
in insurance cases. Conceptually, this is a credible scenario and similar usage is
seen from security companies where guards are equipped with body-worn cameras
to document incidents. Also, the insurance representative, Dorte Palmblad states
that incidents where Vulnerable Road Users (VRU) such as pedestrians, cyclists or
similar are involved, make a terrifying mark on the truck drivers. According to the
statistics of Vejdirektoratet, this is applicable in upwards of one-third of Heavy Goods
Vehicle (HGV) accidents[2]. Per contra, this does not mean that other accidents are
taken with a smile, but accidents, where the road user has no to minimal means of
protection, can be subsequent mind terror for the driver. What could he have done
1
==PAGE:14==
Master Thesis
1. Introduction
diﬀerently? How could he overlook the subject? Where did he go wrong?... Palmblad
believes that she, as an insurance risk agent, could use the video to educate drivers
and potentially provide peace of mind in some cases. As such, the goal of this master
thesis is to assist the start-up company with their vision and develop a high ﬁdelity
prototype of an insurance tool to provide a service for drivers and haulers to be
better protected against false insurance claims or accusations. The prototype will
serve the purpose of validating user experience and the business case through user
perception. The project will further focus on privacy measures as video presents a
concern of privacy issues.
Insurance cases, however, is not the only area where such video could be of interest.
During the lifespan of the project, additional interesting directions with the video
originated and it was chosen to include one as a ’side track’ with the proviso that
it did not trouble the primary objective. The alternative track is to investigate the
potential for the video within the ﬁeld of traﬃc research. Returning to the overall
vision of the start-up behind the data: to aid drivers and haulers, then both tracks
stay with the framework: 1) the insurance perspective and primary objective in the
project can aid drivers and haulers in post-accident scenarios, and 2) the research
perspective and secondary project objective is assumed to aid drivers in pre-accident
scenarios. The state of the latter objective at project termination is depended on
the extend of resources remaining, but a minimum of proposed usage is expected to
be presented.

Report Structure
For simplicity and reader beneﬁt, this report is arranged based on the following
structure:
Background
A review and presentation on subjects related to
the work conducted throughout this project.
Pre-Analysis
Preliminary analysis of the project closely related
to project management.
Analysis
The work made to solve the issues at hand with
the user at the core of the project.
Design
The design of the tool derived from the analysis.
Implementation
Relevant code snippets are presented for discus-
sion.
Tests and Results
The description of ﬁnal tests and experiment used
to validate the business case and their results.
Discussion
Subjects left open during the report are closed in a
discussion where also improvements are suggested.
Conclusion
Project termination and output.

Background 2
In order to initiate any project, it is necessary to know what technology is used, if
diﬀerent technologies are better suited for the project and what alternatives exist.
As such, this chapter will examine subjects related to the business case, the novel
video footage, and the domain from which it is acquired, starting with a presentation
of the partnering company.
2.1
AUGIT Solutions
AUGIT Solutions is a start-up company that originated from the Technical
University of Denmark.
The idea of utilizing AR to eliminate blinds spots
commenced during a brainstorming session in a prototype class where also some
initial tests were conducted by the ﬁve involved students. As the initial experiments
showed promising results, three students from the original group continued and in
late 2018 created the start-up company and expanded with a sales and marketing
proﬁle. In short terms, the company is working to solve one of the problems that
result in traﬃc accidents: the blind spot problem, a problem which is most known
within the domain of HGVs.
Figure 2.1:
Sketch of the solution with
essential components: AR glasses worn by the
HGV driver and external camera. The light-
green ﬁeld highlights the ’see-through’ eﬀect;
The driver can spot the bicycle in the critical
zone. Source: AUGIT Solutions.
The fundamental components of the solution are a pair of AR glasses and external
cameras in an ensemble as roughly displayed by Figure 2.1. The cameras are aimed
towards the critical zones of the truck, and the feed is displayed to the driver through
the glasses. This implies that when drivers e.g., look down to the right, they are
presented with a feeling of the truck turning transparent.
The vision of the start-up company is to aid drivers and thereby save lives in traﬃc.
To bring about this ambition, they have developed their prototype based on the
following description:
5

"Using augmented reality glasses to extend human perception to reveal an-
gles conventionally blinded by the truck chassis. This being accomplished
with an eco-system of sensors and cameras to enhance the driver’s view,
enabling drivers to see through their truck.."

Figure 2.2 sketches the current state of the AUGIT prototype corresponding to the
presented snippet description. As can be observed through comparing the sketch
with the full description in Appendix A, the solution is not ﬁnalized. However,
as the current state of the AUGIT system is independent of the insurance tool
development, this is not considered an obstacle. The project will continue based on
the ﬁnalized product.
2.2
Cameras in Traﬃc
The video footage from AUGIT Solutions’ AR glasses is not the ﬁrst camera solution
introduced to the traﬃc domain. There are various camera solutions with diﬀerent
purposes from surveillance to assistance, some are part of the road network, and
some are in-vehicle.
2.2.1
Built-in Cameras
Cameras that are built into the skeleton of the vehicle or later retroﬁtted to the
vehicle are, in this context, categorized as built-in cameras. Within this category,
there are multiple solutions available for trucks alone incl. CornerEye, MirrorEye,
Rearview, RadarEye, Front view, Side view, SurroundView, BirdsEye view, and so
forth. The general disadvantage of these compared to the AR glasses is that the feed
is visualized through a monitor placed inside the truck, meaning that the truck driver
will remove his line of sight from traﬃc to gain an overview of the traﬃc situation.
To some, this might seem counter-intuitive.
In addition, a report presented by
Havarikommisionen[3] has shown that checking the mirrors alone lies on the border
of human capabilities. Therefore, it could be derived that adding additional screens
can only worsen the eﬀect. It is, however, a learnable skill according to Henning
’Baloo’ Olsen, a truck driver who actively engage in traﬃc safety measurements and
who is the awarded driver of the most secure truck in Denmark 2018.
6
==PAGE:19==
2. Background
DTU
The AUGIT system is what is considered a Body-Worn Camera (BWC) solution.
Although the camera itself is not positioned on the driver, the glasses are, and it is
the glasses that ’controls’ what footage is recorded. If comparing this new solution
to the existing blind spot mitigation techniques, then limited similarities can be
found.

Table 2.1:
Table comparing truck cameras in four aspects: Which angles they cover
(Coverage), What kind of view is used by the driver (View), Where the view is presented
(Display Device), and if warnings of potential collision are included (Warning).
From inspection of Table 2.1 it can immediately be concluded that AUGIT has
the advantage of direct vision as the only solution supported by the image being
displayed directly to the glasses, hence no need to look away from the traﬃc. On the
other hand, AUGIT is seemingly falling behind on coverage where the SurroundView
and BirdEye View (if ignoring the current ﬂaws) fully cover all angles of the truck
and AUGIT is missing the rear end. This is a deliberate choice due to the limitations
of direct sight. When looking far behind with the natural vision, items become
small and therefore hard to detect with the naked eye.
As a solution to this
limitation, zoom eﬀects do exist, and even contact lenses with zoom eﬀect are being
developed[11]. This is untested with traﬃc, and therefor not currently considered
for implementation. As such, for road accidents AUGIT seems the safest choice,
but when parking and backtracking it should be combined with Rear View for full
coverage. By cause of the direct view, the AUGIT video is considered the most
optimal footage to support the driver within an insurance claim.
2.2.2
In-truck Cameras
Built-in cameras for trucks are not the only used tool of visual information for
drivers. DashCams have also made their entrance, which are cameras often placed
in the front shield of a vehicle recording the road ahead. This kind of footage has
become a necessary means of protection in fault claims by drivers and truck drivers
in particular. "If a truck is involved, the past bias has been to blame the truck"
7
==PAGE:20==
Master Thesis
2. Background
- Kim Ancker 1. With DashCams, truck drivers have the opportunity to disprove
the claim if justiﬁed.
The limitation of this technology is the ﬂexibility of the
camera. It is positioned at a ﬁxed place in the front shield only recording in one
direction and only at the road. In an interview with Dorte Palmblad2 she states
that: "..forward external video cannot be used in all circumstances", and provides a
potential scenario:
A hypothetical case
The truck driver needs to turn right and expresses his intention well
in advance by activating the turn signal. He orients himself with the
surroundings and acknowledges that there is a little blue car behind
him. As he turns, he steers the truck towards the left lane in order
to make the turn. The blue car is impatient and overtakes inside.
He moves into the blind spot of the truck and is trapped underneath
as it turns. The blue car states that the turn signal was deactivated
when the truck moved towards the left lane and the truck driver
states the opposite.
The consequence is that in turn and signal accidents, such as the hypothetical case
the fault stays with the truck driver as no evidence exists that says otherwise. This
conclusion is derived from statements of HGV drivers and insurance investigators
and can therefore only be used in that said context. It could not be validated how
criminal matters are resolved or if and how the footage is used in such cases as no
response from police oﬃcials were received during the time of this project despite
multiple attempts.
The DashCam and the AUGIT glasses seem relatively similar in use-cases as they
both ﬁlm the road ahead while driving, however, the glasses are moving with the
head of the driver and not stagnant as for the DashCam. About protecting the
driver against unjustiﬁed guilt claims, it cannot currently be concluded which option
is best. By recording an incident from the driver’s perspective, the quality of the
footage is dependent on the driver, and there is no guarantee that the driver looks
in the ’correct’ direction. On the other hand, if the incident occurs on the side
of the truck, the DashCam cannot record the incident whereas the AUGIT system
can but again, it is dependent on the driver. The question comes down to if a ﬁxed
camera would be better at recording traﬃc incidents or if the driver would be better
at ﬁxating on an incident? An educated guess is ’sometimes’, but nothing validated
as this relies on human emotion, stability, and diversity, which is beyond the scope
of this report.
1Hauler at Kim Ancker Transport
2Insurance representative from CODAN

2. Background
DTU
2.2.3
Traﬃc Cameras
Looking more broadly into the domain of video footage in traﬃc today, traﬃc
cameras are also of great importance. In Denmark there are more than 300 traﬃc
cameras along the road network[12], and though a rich amount it is easily beaten
by the UK where more than 3000 cameras are categorized as traﬃc cameras[13].
Initially, such cameras were used to deliver a real-time eye on highly traﬃcked
roads. With advancements in software development, they can today be considered
a sensory network.
As an example, Omnibond has developed the TraﬃcVision,
a real-time intelligent traﬃc detection system[14]. TraﬃcVision turns any traﬃc
camera into a sensor that can spot real-time accidents and thereby prepare traﬃc
managers to act more eﬃciently, while also able to detect ghost drivers so that
proactive measures can be taken.
Although relevant within the societal developments of today, intelligent systems
are also considered beyond the scope of this project.
The current AUGIT
prototype or future end product speciﬁcations do not include intelligence such as the
TraﬃcVision. This, however, does not mean that intelligent software could not be
implemented at later stages. The car company Nissan launched at CES 2019 their
vision of future technology[15] including AR glasses (software) that can predict if
e.g., a pedestrian is about to cross the road ahead, where parking lots are available
and much more. This provides real-time information to the driver and combined
with travel directions in the glasses, the sight of the driver will reside on the road
(no need to consult external GPS monitor). Having the focus on the road aligns
with the AUGIT vision for road safety. Although not currently pursued by AUGIT,
intelligent features could expand the business case further in the future and also
expand the market appealing to private vehicles as well.
2.3
The Eﬀect of Camera
The recorded AUGIT footage will have one leading role, which is the driver and
perhaps an even more visually displayed supporting actor, the co-driver.
An
immediate concern to the ﬁnal insurance tool is how the presence of a camera will
change people and how they will react.
The Hawthorne eﬀect is one theory within the context of being watched or observed.
It has origin in a research project in Illinois 1924-32 where the objective was to test
if changes in lightning and work structure could increase worker productivity at the
electric company, Hawthorne Works. In the study, as summarized by Wickström and
Bendix[16], they found that the groups exposed to the new conditions were more
productive, however, so were the control group who continued under non-changed
conditions. Subsequently, the Hawthorne eﬀect became a term for the theory stating
that increased attention to the worker needs leads to a temporary increase in worker
9
==PAGE:22==
Master Thesis
2. Background
productivity. As such, increased attention to work by drivers is not likely to yield
negative outcomes concerning accidents and therefore considered a positive quality
of camera presence albeit only temporary.
Multiple studies (e.g., Pilkington et al.[17] and Wilson et al.[18]) have shown how
cameras in traﬃc have changed driver behaviors in terms of the reduced speed at
camera sites. In an article from the University of West of England, it is stated
that "cameras are eﬀective because the public understand how they work (or at least
perceive they do) and know what behaviour is required of them and feel they can
perform such behaviour"[19]. By cause of the transparency behind the intend of the
cameras, it is easy for people to change their behavior. This is again considered a
positive eﬀect.
More in line with the video footage at hand, Ariel, Farrar and Sutherland[20]
conducted a study on the eﬀect of police BWC. They tested how the use of police
force and the number of citizen complaints were eﬀected by police oﬃcer use of
the BWC. In the paper, they conclude that the presence of BWC reduces the
prevalence of use-of-force, i.e. (overstated) that the police are not seeking violent
actions as the ﬁrst measure in confrontations while also decreasing the number of
citizen complaints. Yet another example that the presence of a camera makes people
act diﬀerently in a positive direction.
Negative eﬀects in the literature are not easily found.
In fact, a relevant case
has not been found during this review.
However, the concept of surveillance is
often something encountered when going online, and here, negative associations
are found. In this project, surveillance is categorized into one of two categories:
Voluntary and Involuntary. Voluntary surveillance is associated with, for instance,
home surveillance where people intentionally use cameras in order to feel secure
in their home and protect loved ones[21].
Involuntary is, as the word suggests,
surveillance where the subject has no say. This is being more and more imposed on
workplaces. In an article, BBC interviewed a former bank teller who left her job by
cause of the surveillance being "dehumanizing". Furthermore, it is presented that
in 2020, 80% of companies are expected to use diﬀerent performance surveillance
tools. Though it might seem intrusive Ben Waber at Humanyze explains how the
monitoring can also protect employees from bullying and sexual harassment[22].
Through the eyes of research, cameras and monitoring seem to provide positive
outcomes generally. However, with the word "dehumanizing" weighing heavily in
memory, it is deemed necessary to involve not only the intended users (Hauler and
Insurance) of the insurance platform but also the drivers in the following analysis,
to ensure that they do not acquire the same view on the AUGIT footage.
10
==PAGE:23==
2. Background
DTU
2.4
Data Streams
Data is all around us, and we live in a world where the ever-increasing data volume
is the fuel for innovation. The traﬃc domain is no exception, and supplemental data
to the video can be extracted from various sources. For trucks, the more traditional
data stream originates from the tachograph.
2.4.1
Tachograph
By law trucks are required to have a tachograph; A device ﬁtted to the vehicle
which automatically records data. In 2006 a restriction was made by the European
Union[23], that new trucks should be manufactured with the digital version. This is
now being replaced by the Smart Tachograph which is mandatory from June 15th,
2019[24].
Digital
A digital tachograph records the activities of a driver.
It consists of a piece of
hardware placed in the HGV cabin and is wired to the vehicle itself. The data that
can be extracted from the device is for instance[25, 26]:
Date
Vehicle registration number
Vehicle speed
Driver IDs
Number of times a driver card is inserted each day
Distance travelled by the driver, captured via odometer
Driver activity (driving, rest, breaks, other activities, availability)
Date and time of activity change
Events (speeding, driving without card, tampering, fraud) and errors
Enforcement checks
Details of tachograph calibrations
The device can read 4 diﬀerent types of cards known as smartcards; The driver’s,
the company’s, the workshop’s and the controller’s (Authorities checking the
vehicle)[27].
The smartcards are mandatory digital recorders, used to enforce laws such as the
professional driver’s driving and resting times. The idea is to increase road safety
so that the driver does not exceed the daily driving hours, and to ensure minimum
working condition standards. Furthermore, it can help guarantee fair competition
between EU transport.
11
==PAGE:24==
Master Thesis
2. Background
Smart
The smart tachograph is a new generation of the previous digital tachograph that
implements a bunch of new features. These features make use of satellite, short-
range communication, and interconnection.
The system will also automatically
record the start and end destination of journeys.
The satellite can be used to
gather information about the positioning of the vehicle, whereas the short-range
communication will be used by law enforcement to check a bypassing vehicle’s data
without having to stop it[28].
2.4.2
GNSS Devices
Another possibility for supplemental data can be found through tracking devices.
The Global Navigation Satellite System (GNSS) is a comprised network of satellites
deployed around the world.
The GPS from the USA, GLONASS from Russia,
Galileo from Europe and BeiDou from China are all classiﬁed as GNSS.
In the traditional sense, GNSS devices are not standard equipment in trucks yet.
With the new Smart tachographs this is included with a ping of position every 3
hours[28], however, it has been learned that some haulers equip their trucks with
additional systems for real-time positioning in order to keep track of the shipment
whereabouts. As such, GNSS is considered a potential data source but with the
proviso that not all trucks have one as for now. The data that can be obtained from
these devices is, for instance:
Trip distance
Time, incl time zone
Position
2.4.3
Black Box
By cause warranty claims, many manufacturers started integrating the ’black box’
into their engine components in the 1990s. The black box is formally termed an
Event Data Recorder (EDR) or Electric Control Module (ECM) and is a device
that records the physical state of the truck. In relation to an accident, the personal
injury ﬁrm Brauns Law[29] highlight data such as:
Speed before crash
Deceleration/Acceleration
Cruise control activation
Truck activities
GPS location information
The data on the black box has a limited lifetime to at most 30 days and in some
trucks shorter, afterwards it is recorded over.
12
==PAGE:25==
2. Background
DTU
2.4.4
The AR Glasses
The ﬁnal source considered is the AR glasses themselves. The glasses have two
cameras attached to the front of the device, whereas one is a 1080p RGB camera w/
microphone used for recording and the other is an IR camera used for recognizing
hand gestures[30]. Furthermore, are the device able to track 3 degree-of-freedom,
which refers to rotational movement, and has a sensor responsible for checking
if the AR glasses are equipped.
The device ships with a Unity-based Software
Development Kit (SDK) that supports Windows and Android. The SDK provides
predeﬁned functionality that can be used to interact with the diﬀerent features of
the device. The documentation for the SDK is inadequate as it does not include
how to interact with the sensors or cameras as well as the associated attributes.
The data that can be retrieved from the AR glasses, for instance:
Video recording
Hand gestures
Head movement
Equipment state
Audio

relevant to study how like-minded companies practice to establish a solid foundation
so that the tool can be of general use. For this purpose, the top 5 business insurance
companies in Denmark have been reviewed. These are Tryg, Topdanmark, CODAN,
HDI Global SE, and If who together make up for more than 81% of the Danish
business market as presented in Figure 2.3.

13
==PAGE:26==
Master Thesis
2. Background
Next on the ﬁgure is Topdanmark. Topdanmark is the runner up insurance company
in Denmark and with only a Danish clientele of approx. 620.000 customers[33]. The
practice is similar to that of Tryg with an online formula to ﬁll out after entering a
personal page.
The third-largest business insurance company in Denmark is CODAN. CODAN is a
widespread company with customers in approximately 140 countries and a customer
base of more than 17 million[34]. Once again, the practice here is to ﬁll out an online
formula similar to that of the two formers. However, here you choose your damage
category before logging into your page. A small deviation but conclusively, it is the
same information that is provided for all three companies in a similar manner.
Next is HDI Global SE, formerly known as HDI-Gerling Industrie Versicherung AG.
HDI Global SE is a company with headquarters in Germany and is represented in
more than 150 countries[35]. In contrary to the other presented companies, HDI
Global SE has no private customers; they only supply insurance for businesses.
Furthermore, the report of a damage is also a diﬀerent practice than previously
encountered. Here you can either visit their local branch with address in Copenhagen
or call the local contacts.
Last in this review is If. If is among the leading insurance companies in the Nordic
countries and Baltics with 3.6 million customers[36]. On their website, a familiar,
yet diﬀerent practice is met. It is once again an online formula to be ﬁlled; however,
they use the form of a chat window and a chat-bot to converse with the customer
instead of a paper-like ﬁll-out form. Although a diﬀerent interaction, the underlying
case is the same: go online and provide your information and documentation step
by step.
Through a review of the top 5 insurance companies in Denmark, a norm is identiﬁed.
The general practice in order to report an accident involves the customer to ﬁll out
an online form and attaching evidence in the following formats:
bmp, doc/docx, gif, jpg/jpeg, pdf, rtf, tif, txt, png or xls/xlsx.
The formats present the current barrier when the evidence is video. If the customer
wants to provide video evidence, this is done by mailing the responsible case oﬃcer3.
Hence the request from CODAN to develop an insurance tool where the AUGIT
video can be accessed without having to be stored manually by retrieving it from
an e-mail. Manual storing also provides the pitfall of proper video conduct at the
companies. Video that is no longer of use should not be stored; hence the insurance
agent has the responsibility of deleting the video in their system.

2. Background

2.6
Traﬃc Research
As traﬃc research is introduced as a second objective within this project, it is
likewise relevant to look at existing work related to the video within this perspective.
When looking at the latest statistics from the Institute for Health Metrics and
Evaluation (IHME), road incidents are among the top 20 causes and claim the lives
of 1.35 million people world every year, and it is the number one non-illness cause
in the list.
Figure 2.4:
Annual
number of deaths by
cause in 2017.
Road
incidents
are
the
11th
highest
cause
with
1.35
million
deaths
and
the
ﬁrst
non-illness.
Source:
Institute
for
Health
Metrics
and
Evalua-
tion (IHME)[37]
According to the Global Status Report On Road Safety 2018 by the Worlds Health
Organization (WHO), the number of road incidents continues to climb[38], thereby
making traﬃc road crashes a severe problem. The current trend suggests that the
Sustainable Development Goal to half the traﬃc deaths by 2020 is unobtainable,
and instead, road incidents are predicted to become the seventh leading cause of
death in 2030[39]. So what is the greatest sinner in road incidents?
In 1979 J.R. Treat et al.[40] conducted a study at the Transportation Research
Institute of Michigan including 2258 traﬃc investigations where they concluded
that upwards of 92.6% of the accidents were caused by human factors. The factors
were divided into ﬁve categories: improper lookout (23%), excessive speed (17%),
inattention (15%), improper evasive action (13%), and internal distraction (9%).
A more recent study published in 2017 by S.P. Santosa et al.[41] showed similar
results with driver inﬂuence as the immense cause of traﬃc accidents; In 87.57% of
the investigated 28000 fatalities, human factors were concluded the dominant cause.
Further details showed the root elements in careless driving, speeding, and unruly
behavior.
Human factors in driving are often examined in two diﬀerent domains: Driving skills
and driver behaviors. Driving skill is also referred to as driving performance, which
involves information processing and skills in general. Driver behavior, or driving
style as it is also referred to, express habits among drivers.
15
==PAGE:28==
Master Thesis
2. Background
2.6.1
Driver Behaviour
WHO has proposed three keys to accelerating the progress to lessen the number
of road incidents[38]:
1) standards for roads and vehicles, 2) mitigating high-
risk behaviors, and 3) ensuring timely access to emergency care. Keys 1 and 3,
for the sake of this report, are considered on governmental levels for acceleration
as they require legislation and ﬁnancial backing.
Key 2, however, is related to
driving behavior where researchers have made great eﬀort in order to understand
the mindset of drivers.
In 1990 Reason et al. developed the Driver Behaviour Questionnaire (DBQ)[42],
which is one of the most used appliances for measuring driver behavior in relation
to collision risk. A meta-analysis of the predictability of the DBQ showed it utilized
in at least 174 studies in 2010 by Dodou and de Winter[43]. The original DBQ
consists of 50 items4 within three main behavioral categories: Slips and lapses,
Mistakes, and Deliberate violations.
The authors acknowledge the limitations of
the DBQ in that the results are based upon subjective impressions of past driving
experience - impressions that are several stages removed from the actual incidents.
However, the results found in the study corresponded reasonably well with views
obtained through other measures, and because of its simplicity, it is well adopted as
a behavior measurement tool in regards to driver behavior.
Additional research (e.g., Hatﬁeld et al., 2017[44]) show that impulsivity is an
inﬂuential factor when regarding driving outcome and behavior.
The general
structure of impulsivity is conceptualized as having three dimensions[45]:
• motor impulsiveness (urgency):
i.e. acting without thinking
• cognitive/attentional impulsiveness (perseverance):
i.e. diﬃculty in focusing on the current task and making quick decisions
• nonplanning (premeditation):
i.e. lack of future orientation and consequences of actions
As alluded by the dimensions, impulsiveness is often concluded to be a negative and
dysfunctional trait in the context of driver behavior. In order to understand the
link between this maladaptation and driving behavior, researchers have sought to
develop tools to access the driving context-speciﬁc impulsivity. One such tool is the
Impulsive Driver Behavior Scale (IDBS) by Bıçaksız and Özkan[45]. Their study
included the development and test of the measurement tool which was concluded
to have good psychometric properties that show "promise in enhancing our ability
to study and understand the psychological factors associated with driving related
incidents"[45, p. 355].
4There were three sections in total: Sec. 1 was ten questions about personal details, Sec. 2
was 50 items for analyzing behavior, Sec. 3 was ﬁve questions for general self-assessment.
16
==PAGE:29==
2. Background
DTU
Research within the ﬁeld of driving behavior has been going on for decades in
which various methods have been developed not only limited to the few described.
Methods such as self-reported behavior and risk preferences (Goldenbeld and Van
Schagen, 2007), creating demographic proﬁles (Wundersitz and Hutchinson, 2008)
and personality and risk perceptions (Manchin and Plint, 2010) also sought to
explain diﬀerent aspects of unsafe driving practices and the list continues. Common
for these methods is that they generally rely only on small sample size and assume
homogeneity of behavior resulting in over or under-predicting road safety outcomes.
As such, more recent studies utilize the wast amount of data accessible through
the vehicles to develop more accurate measures about the driver. In 2015, Ellison
et al.[46] developed the concept of Driver Behaviour Proﬁles (DBP) based on
naturalistic driving data-sets. The DBPs are generated through potentially millions
of observations for each driver collected during their day-to-day driving, thereby
measuring driver behavior across time and space. The outcome of the proposed
DBP framework is a score of driver behavior that serves as a measure for assessing
crash risk and can also be adopted to inform drivers of their tendencies in order to
inspire self-improvement. The authors conclude that the DBP is a ﬂexible framework
that can be adapted to multiple parameters, and could beneﬁt from e.g., video.
2.6.2
Driving Skills
Research is also often used in link with education. A recent study at DTU showed
how simulator driving training with eye-tracking can improve the Hazard Perception
Skill (HPS) for young drivers. The author, Abele, suggests developing advanced
HPS among new drivers to decrease pedestrian-related hazards[47].
In relation
to training, Force Technology completed in 2017 a training tool based on videos
recorded in an almost identical manner to that of AUGIT. They recorded various
videos from four diﬀerent truck driving scenarios using eye-tracking glasses: 1) Right
turns, 2) Left turns, 3) Roundabout, and 4) Straight driving. These videos were
afterward published on the YouTube channel "ANVENDT PSYKOLOGI - FORCE
Technology" for training oﬃcials to use as supplements in their education of drivers
but also intended for the public to be made aware of how much visual attention
brings to road safety[48].
2.6.3
Data Acquisition
When looking beyond driver behaviors or driving skills, a general problem for traﬃc
accident research is highlighted by Møller et al. to be the available data or rather
where to ﬁnd the data. By comparing the National Patient Registry to the oﬃcial
accident statistics in Denmark, they found that, in 2017, only 5% of cyclists who had
incurred an injury were registered accordingly[49]. ’Missing’ data can have several
17
==PAGE:30==
Master Thesis
2. Background
consequences; The data cannot be used in contribution to research on the inﬂuence of
ambulance arrival, it cannot be used to provide knowledge about alcohol accidents,
and so forth. Data is the source of research advancements, and the more data,
the more accurate predictions can be made. Therefore, missing data is potentially
causing harm by not having been a variable in the latest research.
2.6.4
Scoping
Traﬃc research is a broad spectrum of possibilities and too broad for it all to
be considered within this project.
Education is proven to beneﬁt from videos.
However, the eye-tracking video is superior to the AUGIT video in this scenario
as pupil ﬁxations can illustrate what a driver is overseeing, which can then be
used in subsequent training or discussions. Nonetheless, education is an interesting
perspective, but none followed in this report based on the current functionality of the
glasses. Instead, common for most behavioral research is that it either relies on what
the driver remembers such as the DBQ and IDBS or it relies on the digital perception
the sensory system of the car provides as for the DBP. With new technology such
as the AUGIT glasses being introduced to the traﬃc domain, existing data can
be complemented with the actual sight of the driver.
This could bring greater
details to e.g., the DBQ and more data on traﬃc accidents as required for optimal
predictions. In addition, it could potentially bring about a better understanding
for drivers about their behavior than a score as provided by the DBP. Returning to
the WHO ﬁndings, that mitigation of high-risk behavior could decrease the number
of traﬃc casualties, this perspective in yet unexplored with video. As such, this
investigation will continue to examine the possibilities for the AUGIT video within
the domain of driving behavior and general accident research.

The project is, in the most basic sense, about developing

available to whom it may interest with a ﬁrst target
being insurance companies.

18
==PAGE:31==
2. Background
DTU
David Benyon writes that UX ".. encompasses all feelings, thoughts, sensations,
and actions of engaging in some activity"[51], highlighting that there are more
to user experience than the user.
To embrace this perspective, the project will
proceed as human-centered, i.e., putting people ﬁrst, but not reduce the importance
of activity, context, and technology in the process. This technique will be adopted
to understand the breadth of the future solution and used to promote good UX as
users will seek elsewhere if the service is not easily operated or understood.
2.7.1
Usability
Easy to use, easy to learn, ﬂexible. That is some of the words used to describe
usability. Proper implementation of this component would remove the barriers just
described that could make people seek other services. However, there is more to it.
Benyon also reﬂects on Accessibility and Acceptability as equally key components.
Before a system is usable, it must be accessible, and for people to continue using the
product, it must be acceptable. Benyon’s take on usability is comparable to that of
ISO 9241-11 where the components eﬀectiveness, eﬃciency, and satisfaction are the
keys to quality[52] but he takes it one step further and stresses the notion of e.g.,
acceptability in order to have a successful product. Also, as this project seeks to
validate the potential for the AUGIT business case through user perceptions, the
usability component of satisfaction is emphasized. As such, usability tests with base
in the ISO standard will be conducted but with an extended focus on satisfaction
and acceptability. For satisfaction, the Elements of Value Pyramid can be used
to emphasize how the product or service supports the need of the user and how it
creates value. The pyramid is constructed to address four kinds of needs: functional,
emotional, life changing, and social impact. There are 30 elements in total, some of
which are inwardly focused, and some are outward.
As will be later conferred, there are multiple stakeholders in the current project,
and accordingly, it is expected for there to be multiple preferences, needs, and goals
to consider. Some might even be contradicting, which can be complicated to juggle
without understanding how human behavior is shaped. One possible answer is found
in the dual-process theories.
2.7.2
Dual Process Theories
One important aspect to consider in order to understand human behavior is the
dual process. There are several theories diﬀerentiated in the details; however, they
share the basic concept in that people have two modes of thinking: automatic and
reﬂective. The automatic mode is estimated to be used in 95% of daily decisions
and is the dominant part of decisions with minimal eﬀort, quick decisions, and the
like. It is described as being instinctive, emotional, and operates unconsciously[53].
19
==PAGE:32==
Master Thesis
2. Background
The reﬂective, on the other hand, is conscious, slow, and goal-oriented. During the
dominant mode, automatic, heuristics are applied to enable fast decisions. One such
example is the herd instinct where decisions are based upon what other people do.
While optimal in many situations, heuristic support also introduces biases: People
often tend to follow the default option, as it is the path of least resistance, rather
than reﬂect on alternatives. Caraban et al. provide the example of organ donation:
Example of status-quo bias
"..several countries in Europe have changed their laws to make organ donation
the default option. In such so called opt-out contexts, over 90% of the citizens
donate their organs; while in opt-in contexts the rate falls down to 15%."[53]
From a design point of view, this is valuable knowledge. The default action should be
highly considered during the design of the platform and also in how to interact with
the glasses. This form of system development where the designer ’takes advantage’
of the automatic mode of thinking is associated with nudging.
2.7.3
Nudging
Knowing biases in decision making provides the necessary mean of leverage in order
to support people in making ’the right’ choice. This is termed nudging and deﬁned
by Thaler and Sunstein as "any aspect of the choice architecture that alters people’s
behavior in a predictable way without forbidding any options or signiﬁcantly changing
their economic incentives"[54]. The deﬁnition aligns with the previous example of
organ donation where opt-in was changed with an opt-out policy, but the option
remains.
In addition to the two modes of automatic and reﬂective thinking, Hansen and
Jespersen expanded the model with the dimension of nudge transparency, as
presented in Figure 2.6, in order to formalize nudging strategies.
Figure 2.6: Four categories of nudges: interpretation of Hansen and
Jespersens Intervention Types[55]
20
==PAGE:33==
2. Background
DTU
The level of transparency denotes if the user can identify the objective behind the
nudge. To promote the understanding of the division, some examples are provided:
• Inﬂuencing Behaviour: (Automatic-Transparent)
e.g. change the default option.
• Prompting of Reﬂected Choice: (Reﬂective-Transparent)
e.g. "keep right - it’s the opposite of left" campaign on the Danish roads.
• Manipulating Behaviour: (Automatic-Non Transparent)
e.g. rearrange the lunch tables to seat more people to facilitate talk with more
colleagues, hence better solutions.
• Manipulating Choice: (Reﬂective-Non Transparent)
e.g. provide less relevant options to a set of choices to increase the value of
the desired choice.
The intervention types framework provides a guide to the design of the system
where some stakeholder conﬂicts can potentially be solved. Based on the article by
Caraban et al. ﬁve of the six presented nudging categories were found to beneﬁt the
current project in terms of interaction:
Facilitate, Confront, Deceive, Social Inﬂuence, Fear, Reinforce
During the review of the article, no mechanisms within the Deceive category were
found suitable for the current project and will not be further considered. The exact
inﬂuence of the remaining categories and their position in terms of the intervention
types framework will be described later during the Design (Chapter 5).
21
==PAGE:34==
==PAGE:35==
Pre-Analysis 3
Many drivers are blamed for being the cause of accidents every year. In most cases,
this is considered to be accurate and as such a bias has been formed "to blame the
big guy". It is, however, not true in all cases but by cause of the bias, the drivers
are often blamed as they have no means of evidence except their word. In these
cases, insurance agents have a job comparable to that of a lawyer. It is their job to
defend their clients in the best way possible, but as in a criminal trial, these cases
are rarely won without evidence.
There is a great amount of data generated from the trucks, and even more can
be obtained with supplements such as video.
With video recorded from the
drivers perspective, the driver will no longer be subject to the group opposition
of "six against one" as stated by a claims agent at CODAN. No evidence exists
to support the driver and make the job of an insurance agent less complicated
as case management can become more objective and based on concrete evidence
rather than ﬁrst or second-hand witnesses. Being as it may, this data is currently
not accessible for insurance agents. In order to address this pain, the proceeding
chapter will present the goals within this project, and methodologies followed in
order to achieve a successful output.
3.1
Goals
With respect to the AUGIT (and CODAN) request, this project is shaped so that it
beneﬁts from the skills of the authors. The thesis description, presented in Appendix
B, provides the high-level portrayal of the project from where the following goal
has been formed: to develop a high-ﬁdelity prototype for sharing the AUGIT video
with supplemental data to resolve the problem of evidence accessibility. From this,
the main objective is believed to be obtainable, to validate the business case by
answering the following question:
Main Objective
Is there a desire for such tool to be realized for
insurance agents and haulers, respectively?
23
==PAGE:36==
Master Thesis
3. Pre-Analysis
As the analysis will show, the project output took a turn from being an extension to
the existing systems at insurance companies to being an online platform from where
to access the video. However, the overall goal is the same. In terms of validation,
this can be obtained on multiple levels. An ideal case would be to evaluate the
outcome of a realized full-scale platform: Does the insurance agents and haulers use
the tool?. However, as an outcome is evaluated after project termination based on
the achievements of the provided service, this is not viable due to time constraints.
Therefore, in this case, the success will be evaluated on the user’s perception of the
project output through observations during usability tests and interviews.
When validating a start-up business case, there are numerous recommendations on
how to proceed. In the online community Startup.com, it is recommended to get
a minimum of two validations in each customer segment. The unknown ’expert’
states that more time used on validation can be a waste but adds: "The more
people you ask, the more conﬁdent you can be that the answers you’re getting are
representative of your market, and that there are no confounding variables lurking
in the background and skewing your results"[56]. As such, the conducted validations
have to be performed with at least two users, but it is acknowledged that when
relying on such low numbers, it is important to be critical with the results.
In addition, it can be speculated if the tool can be realized at all in terms of law or
ethical aspects as it is built upon sharing the video. Since the desire for the tool is
insigniﬁcant if the possibility of the product does not exist, this must be clariﬁed,
and privacy aspects must be evaluated. With this in mind and in the interest of
overview, the project is broken down into more manageable pieces, and the following
high-level goals are established:
Understand legal limitation
Understand user needs
Develop a prototype platform to access video
Test desire of the platform
Evaluate for security aspects
Document project progress and results
Table 3.1: High-level goals to be achieved in order to conﬁrm project success.
With the incentive to validate if the platform is a desirable pursue for the start-up
company based on user perception, a logical strategy is to proceed with a user-
centered perspective involving users, customers, and data providers. The project is
initiated through a request to use the AUGIT video to aid drivers in fault claims.
This is the primary objective; however, the project is undertaken to be open-minded
to the use of the data as long as it supports the overall vision of helping truck drivers.
Therefore, a perspective in the form of using the video within the context of research
is added as a secondary objective.

3.3
Risks
This project is unique in the sense that no prior platform for video evidence sharing
has been made available for haulers and insurance companies. By Harvey Maylor
this is termed a ’ﬁrst-timer’[60], and being a ﬁrst-timer indicates higher risk and
uncertainty.
Risk and uncertainty can be fatal to any project if not managed
properly. To reduce the negative impact of risks, a risk analysis has been conducted
in order to carry out a plan that takes reservations for potential implications. The
following lists the risks identiﬁed at project initiation:
1. Communication limitations with stakeholders
a) insurance, b) hauler, c) drivers, d) AUGIT, e) researchers
2. Bias from data subject (driver)
3. Legal limitations
4. Equipment damages
5. Equipment availability
6. Unknown complexity of provided software
7. Skills in development environment
3.3.1
Risk Evaluation
The seven identiﬁed risks have been evaluated in terms of estimated impact and
probability of occurring in the project.
1. The stakeholders involved in this project are business professionals which
means that they prioritize their own company responsibilities ahead of being
available for the project.
That was the terms set by the stakeholders
contacted before project initiation. Furthermore, there is uncertainty as to
how additional stakeholders are willing to engage in the project. The impact
of missing stakeholder communication is high as the project is depended on
user validation.
The probability, in general, is considered high but varies
depended on prior contact.
28
==PAGE:41==
3. Pre-Analysis
DTU
2. Although AUGIT prior to this project has interacted with drivers with positive
feedback, it is unknown how the drivers will react about their ﬁeld of view
being recorded.
The impact of negative attitude from drivers are high as
the ﬁnal platform is depended on the video, but for the development of the
platform, this is of low impact. The probability is estimated low.
3. The video recordings will contain personal information about people and
vehicles within the drivers’ ﬁeld of view. It is assumed that there will be law
limitations; however, the consequences for the platform are currently unknown.
The probability of law limitations that cannot be solved is low. The impact,
on the other hand, is considered high.
4. AUGIT has provided one pair of AR glasses for development and testing.
Being an early-stage start-up, funding is limited, and no new device can be
purchased doing this project.
The impact of damaging the glasses is low
concerning platform development, and probability is estimated to be medium.
5. The start-up company has limited equipment indicating, that should they need
the glasses to return, they must be made available. In relation to developing
the platform, this has no impact. The probability is estimated to medium as
one pair of glasses broke during AUGIT’s last user test.
6. Unknown complexity is considered a great risk for planning as it brings high
uncertainty towards respective tasks. As such, this can have a high impact
and is estimated accordingly. For probability, this is estimated as medium as
AUGIT has agreed to be available for consulting.
7. AUGIT has expressed their desire of using C# as the development language.
With prior experience in C#, the probability is valued low where the impact
is valued medium.
When placing the risks according to evaluated weight expressed as low-medium-high
on a 1-3 scale, the probability/impact matrix is as presented in Figure 3.3.

3.3.2
Risk Management
The identiﬁed risks presented in Figure 3.3 are directly translated according to the
ARTA model of risk management. The derived management strategies are presented
accordingly in Figure 3.4.
Figure
3.4:
Strategies to administer
risks:
1) Avoid that risk becomes a
threat, 2) Reduce probability and/or the
impact of threat, 3) Transfer impact and
management of risk to someone else, and
4) Accept risk as part of the risk index and
deal with it if necessary.
Risks 1 is translated into the AVOID category. In order to manage risks associated
with missing communication, a strategy is to interact with stakeholders known by
AUGIT and utilizing the network around them and the university. Thereby avoid
spending time on stakeholder acquisition, and meetings can be put in place as a
ﬁrst step in the project to avoid planning issues.
Risks 2 is translated into the REDUCE category. In order to mitigate the probability
of risk 2, early interaction with the drivers will be planned so that concerns can be
clariﬁed.
Risks 3 and 6 fall under the TRANSFER category. Abstractly, the legal aspects of
risk 3 can be said to be transferred to later stages in the development as they are
not a direct threat to the scope of this project. However, it is still important to
investigate legal aspects in order for the prototype platform to be realistic. Risk
6, concerning unknown software complexity, is likewise transferred to the providing
company as the validation of the platform can proceed without implementing the
existing AR module software.
Lastly, risk 4, 5, and 7 are categorized under ACCEPT. The possibility of proceeding
the project without the AR glasses, as stated by risk 4 and 5, is accepted, and a
contingency plan is produced should it be the case. If the glasses are not available,
a prerecorded video can be made to validate the concept to some extent. Inherent
short-comings within the development language is accepted similarly.
Should it
prove to be more diﬃcult than expected, another language can be utilized in order
to validate the platform.

3.5
Project Planning
Being that features of the platform are currently unknown and both time and costs
for the project are ﬁxed, an agile approach is undertaken. To weaken the impact
of inadequate task estimations, the project is divided into ’sprints’. The adopted
methodology will adhere to that of SCRUM with adjustments. From the insurance
perspective, CODAN will represent the product owner. However, a representative
from the hauler perspective has not been established, and therefore, diﬀerent haulers
may be present during the life span of the project based on availability.
By cause of limited information, project planning will proceed as that of Rolling
Wave where details are added as they materialize and each sprint increment is
considered a milestone roughly corresponding to the high-level goals. The sprints
will commence with prioritizing tasks to add to the sprint backlog and proceed as
presented in Figure 3.5.
Figure 3.5: Iterative approach followed within each sprint
As the ﬁrst step in each sprint, a plan will be constructed.
Changes and new
features will then be designed and implemented as part of development, later tested
and evaluated based on feedback. This process is repeated until the end of the sprint
where learnings will be transferred to the following sprint, e.g., for better planning.
This is comparable with a diﬀerent method known from lean methodology where
31
==PAGE:44==
Master Thesis
3. Pre-Analysis
the circle of activities is centered similarly for continues improvement.
In turn,
this reduces risks associated with the project and advance knowledge for upcoming
sprints and further promotes the ﬂow of value to the users of the system[61].
The scope of this project is mostly concerned with user perception of the system.
Therefore, the quality of the increments is based on feedback rather than quality
control. The presented method is intended as the overall approach. Being as it may,
the initial sprints are dedicated to research where development is not concerned with
the implementation of features but to obtain necessary knowledge for later stages.
This report will reﬂect the ﬁndings in a linear portrayal for reader beneﬁts.

Analysis 4
With a foundation in human-centered development, this chapter presents the
advancements made during the project in order to mature the business case with
the concept of interaction at its core.
The analysis seeks to understand what
requirements are needed in order to create a successful product from the perspective
of users as well as legal necessities. Based on the requirements, design analyses are
conducted concerning tests, prototypes, and security measures.
4.1
Field Observations
AUGIT Solutions was showcasing its prototype1 at Herning Transport 2019, where
the opportunity was given to converse with the current customer base and observe
user interactions. Although, at the time being, the case of data subject acceptance
was considered validated by AUGIT a follow up was performed by cause of the
signiﬁcance to this project. The follow up was performed from both a qualitative
and a quantitative angle. The qualitative method adopted was an interview, with
mainly drivers as respondents, as a tool to understand people’s attitudes.
The
question of data sharing was closed but supplemented with open questions for deeper
understanding. The interview guide for the semi-structured interview can be found
in Appendix E. The quantitative approach was in the form of a questionnaire with
the same exploratory purpose of understanding the attitudes of the data subjects
to support the AUGIT conclusion.
4.1.1
Interviews
During the ﬁrst of the three days at Transport 2019, 12 short interviews were
conducted with participants who had just tried the AUGIT prototype.
The
respondents were ten males and two females between the ages of 24 to 62 (mean
= 42, std = 11)2. The interviews were kept to a few questions related to the view
presented by the solution. In accordance with the questionnaire, all were asked if
they would share the recorded video. The answers provided by the ten drivers, Leif

4.1.2
Questionnaire
The questionnaire can be found in Appendix F and contains ten questions related
to driver perception of sharing data. In Herning, the questionnaire was printed on
paper in order for no drivers to be ’intimidated’ by answering online, hence the
limitation of ten questions.
It was placed at an unsupervised high table at the
entrance to the booth, which resulted in 48 answers. The participants included 41
males and seven females in the range of 18-70 years old (mean=40, std=13). Of
the 48, 31 participants were currently employed as drivers, two retired drivers, two
haulers, ﬁve engineers, two consultants, one market responsible, and one materialist.
The remaining four did not register employment or profession.
One noticeable result from the questionnaire was that 37.5% of the drivers had been
in an accident, as presented in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Distribution of the 48 participant answers to the question "Have you been
involved in a traﬃc accident?" with three response options: 1) Yes, with several involved
(20.8%), 2) Yes, a solo accident (16.7%), and 3) No (62.5%)
This is considered a rather signiﬁcant value based on the low count of accidents
in Denmark. In 2015, ’only’ 154 trucks were involved in an accident[62] and there
were more than 28000 registered trucks in Denmark[63]. In reality, these numbers
cannot be compared as the questionnaire illustrates the percentage of drivers in an
accident throughout their whole carrier, whereas the provided statistics are based
on one year and trucks in Denmark, not Danish drivers. A direct comparison for the
questionnaire was not found, but the given statistics provide the necessary means

4.1.3
Outcome from AUGIT Showcase
During the event, the authors were convinced of there being no problem with the
success of the platform. In this case, success being based on data availability and
that the drivers would freely be the data source and provide the videos.
This
assumption was based on the 12 short interviews conducted during the exhibition.
However, when returning to the oﬃce looking at the contradictory answers provided
through the questionnaire, this conﬁdence was lowered.
There are several possibilities for the answers to be contradictory. The interview
answers were all positive which could be caused by the participating subjects not
speaking their honest opinions based on the face-to-face nature of this interview
conforming to the demand characteristic; The subjects took the role of the good-
participants in order not to cause ’ruin’ within the experiment. The questionnaire,
on the other hand, were placed at a high table, giving the possibility for the
participants to answer unsupervised. Another possibility for the contradiction could
be the knowledge of the participants in relation to BWC. It is known that all
interview participants had tried the solution and were shown the feed which would
be recorded. This cannot be said with certainty for the questionnaire participants.
As the questionnaire was placed before the truck where the system was set-up, it
could be reasoned that these participants answered before knowing the kind of BWC
video it referred to. Furthermore, in hindsight, it is evident that the related question
in the questionnaire was formulated with the expectation that the participants had
knowledge about existing BWC solutions which can not be guaranteed.
Without data being available, the platform the validation of the desire is
insigniﬁcant. As such, the issue of data subject contradictions must be resolved, and
an unambiguous answer must be found. This requires further validation than ﬁrst
anticipated, which will play a key role in later product realization. As a consequence,
the scope is expanded to obtain validation from not just users of the platform but
now also users of the glasses. This will be further elaborated in Section 4.5 where the
test design is analyzed. An equally important aspect to consider is legal restrictions
as they can likewise be the cause of product failure if not correctly identiﬁed.
36
==PAGE:49==
4. Analysis
DTU
4.2
Legal Aspects
The video footage will be ﬁlming on public roads as it reﬂects what the driver sees.
Therefore, in order to develop any platform, whether for insurance claims or research
purposes, it is important to explore what is legally possible.
4.2.1
General Data Protection Regulation
Data protection has become of special interest with the introduction of the General
Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) eﬀective from May 25th 2018.
GDPR is a
legislation introduced by the EU, which concerns handling, managing, and being in
control of personal data within any company. In order to gain an overview, experts
recommend to obtain knowledge of the following questions[64]:
• Do you process personal information?
• What personal information are you treating?
• Why are you processing your personal information?
• How do you process the personal information?
• Where do you process personal information?
• How long do you process the personal information?
These questions will be revisited and sought answered at a later stage when all
facets of the data are known. In preparation, it is necessary to deﬁne the concept
of ’personal data’ in relation to GDPR. The regulation provides the following
deﬁnition:
‘personal data’ means any information relating to an identiﬁed
or identiﬁable natural person (‘data subject’); an identiﬁable
natural person is one who can be identiﬁed, directly or
indirectly, in particular by reference to an identiﬁer such as
a name, an identiﬁcation number, location data, an online
identiﬁer or to one or more factors speciﬁc to the physical,
physiological, genetic, mental, economic, cultural or social
identity of that natural person;
Table 4.1: ’Personal data’ deﬁnition by GDPR: Chapter 1, Article 4, Paragraph (1)[65]
As such, personal data refers to all information about a person, whether direct or
indirect, that can lead to the identiﬁcation of that said person. A second essential
deﬁnition in order to answer the above questions is the term ’processing’ which in
GDPR reads:
37
==PAGE:50==
Master Thesis
4. Analysis
‘processing’ means any operation or set of operations which
is performed on personal data or on sets of personal data,
whether or not by automated means, such as collection, record-
ing, organisation, structuring, storage, adaptation or alter-
ation, retrieval, consultation, use, disclosure by transmission,
dissemination or otherwise making available, alignment or
combination, restriction, erasure or destruction
Table 4.2: ’Processing’ deﬁnition by GDPR: Chapter 1, Article 4, Paragraph (2)[65]
For this project, with video footage as a primary data source, recording and storing
are of great interest and according to GDPR deﬁned as processing data.
With
an understanding of the terms ’personal data’ and ’processing’ concerning GDPR,
further inspection can be conducted.
Rights of the data subject
The data subject is characterized as the person whom the data belongs to, i.e., the
person (or persons) that is portrayed in the data. With the video, this concerns the
driver and the co-driver if one exists. When processing personal data, it is necessary
to obtain written consent from the data subject. Otherwise, the processing is illegal
with respect to GDPR.
The controller shall take appropriate measures to provide any
information ... to the data subject in a concise, transparent,
intelligible and easily accessible form, using clear and plain
language,... The information shall be provided in writing...
Table 4.3: Transparency: Chapter 3, Article 12, Paragraph (1)[66]
In conjunction with the consent, it is necessary for the controller to state the nature
of the processing in a manner that is understandable in writing, unless requested
otherwise by the data subject. In continuation of Article 12 in Table 4.3, Article 13
and 14 continues with the list of information that must be provided at the time the
data are obtained. For simplicity the articles are rewritten into key points:
... where personal data are collected from the data subject
Article 13
§ 1
(a) + (b)
Contact details of controller and protection oﬃcer
(c)
The purpose of processing
(c)
Legal basis of processing
(d)

Table 4.4: Summarized information to provide for data-subjects: Chapter 3, Article 13

In addition to Table 4.4, GDPR address’ aspects to uphold after collection of data
which includes informing the data subject if the intent is changed and the like. From
the research perspective, the paragraphs of Article 14 are annulled by paragraph 5b
being for "...research purposes"[68]. The essential part of the rights of the data
subject is boiled down to being transparent as a controller and willing to provide
any data about the subject for inspection, deletion or other requests made by the
subject.
Principles
Processing of personal data revealing racial or ethnic origin,
political opinions, religious or philosophical beliefs, or trade
union membership,
and the processing of genetic data,
biometric data for the purpose of uniquely identifying a
natural person, data concerning health or data concerning
a natural person’s sex life or sexual orientation shall be
prohibited.
Table 4.5: ’Processing of special categories of personal data’ by GDPR: Chapter 2, Article
9, Paragraph 1[69]
The objective of this project is to utilize the AR footage from the drivers perspective.
As such, the video footage will reveal multiple aspects mentioned in Art. 9 §1 both
about the driver and people on the road and the street. However, when the data
is used for "...scientiﬁc or historical research purposes", §1 is annulled by §2 (j).
Again, this resolves any issues concerning the data for a research platform but not
for the video being used in fault claims. As such, two options remain within this
perspective:
39
==PAGE:52==
Master Thesis
4. Analysis
(1) To collect written consent from all identiﬁable people in the presented footage
in compliance with Art. 7[70].
(2) To blur all personal information in the footage, so no personal data described
in Art. 9 (Table 4.5) can be identiﬁed.
4.2.2
Criminal Code
A helping hand in regards to overcoming the task of blurring all personal data for
fault claims can be found in the Danish Criminal Code (straﬀeloven) chapter 27
§264d.
... the person who improperly discloses messages or images relating
to someone else’s private aﬀairs or otherwise pictures of the person
in question in circumstances which may be apparently required to
evade the public is punished. The provision also applies where the
message or image relates to a deceased person.
Table 4.6: Peace violations and defamation, Chap. 27 §264d translated from Danish[71]
The former lawyer and now director at SikkerhedsBranchen, Kasper Skov-Mikkelsen,
during an interview, interpreted this as "blurring is not needed if the images are not
made public". By cause of this interpretation, it is derived that the video can be
used in fault claims without the task of blurring data if the people involved sign
a consent form with a clause not to share the images further.
This also aligns
with the standard practice today where insurance companies do not collect consent
from every individual that is in the video they receive as evidence in a claim. The
interview notes can be found in Appendix H.
4.2.3
TV Surveillance Law
The General Data Protection Regulation is not the only law to consider
when working with video footage.
The Danish TV Surveillance Law (TV-
overvågningsloven) should also be considered which dictates how and where
surveillance is legal. If categorized as TV surveillance by Danish law, then further
restrictions must be applied and adhered to, e.g., one cannot ﬁlm on public roads.
The deﬁnition of TV surveillance is:
...continuous or periodically repeated person surveillance by
remote or automatic TV camera, photographic apparatus or
similar device.
The Act’s rules on television surveillance
apply correspondingly to the setting up of such apparatus for
monitoring purposes..
Table 4.7: TV-surveillance, Paragraph 1 psc. 2 translated from Danish[72]
40
==PAGE:53==
4. Analysis
DTU
In order to bypass the additional restrictions that come with the TV Surveillance
Law, the system must not be categorized as automatic. This indicates that the
driver has to take an eﬀective action for the recording to commence.
4.2.4
Further International Considerations
Every national government has its own set of laws that apply to the given country.
The project platform should, therefore, respect and comply with these laws such
that no violations are encountered now, or in the future. If the recording feature
of the AR glasses is prohibited in a passing country, the need for the glasses to
stop recording while still serving its original purpose is essential. Furthermore, can
governments require that data gathered within their jurisdiction has to be stored
in data-centers physically located in their respective country[73]. In order to be
compliant, AUGIT should consider controlling a server in each of these countries
where the recordings can be uploaded before leaving the country.
4.3
Requirements
With an understanding of restrictions within the law, it is time to advance to user
requirements. It has been stated that the project aims to develop a prototype of
readiness level 6. By having a prototype at this stage, it is assumed that more
accurate test results can be obtained as the prototype can be tested in a real-
life environment (although a simulated scenario).
As such, the focus is aimed
towards establishing requirements to fulﬁll user needs in terms of the platform.
The requirements are based upon user stories formed during interviews with relevant
stakeholders within the three groups of users: Hauler, Insurance, and Research. The
user stories are part of the human-centered approach for the development of the data
platform and created to support an agile process. For simplicity and consistency,
the user stories are listed by the model presented by Mike Cohn[74]:
As a <type of user>,
I want <some goal>
so that <some reason>
The following user stories are based on the assumption that both objectives
(evidence sharing and research), are concluded appropriate for development.
In
addition, personas are created to support the human-centered design further. The
personas are created in collaboration with users or derived from interviews and
observations. In total, seven personas are developed as a rough representative of
the encountered user types:

4.3.1
Hauler Perspective
To understand user needs from the hauler perspective, two ﬁeld expeditions were
made in order to interview and observe the haulers in their environment.
Kim
Ancker is the hauler at the company of the same name (Kim Ancker Transport).
Although still a hauler, Ancker and his employee Henning ’Baloo’ Olsen dedicate
much of their time to educate about traﬃc safety. During a conversation with Ancker
he was asked how he could imagine himself providing the video for his insurance
company:
"... You just click the video and type the name of whom you
want to share it with (Just like Facebook)." - Ancker
A Facebook equivalent feature for video sharing was the main take away from Ancker
or rather a feature just as simple as that of Facebook sharing. Also, Ancker suggests
using the video in personal improvements of the drivers. As such, a feature of ﬁnding
all videos from a speciﬁc driver is established.
A second hauler was interviewed to create a more comprehensive list of requirements
before engaging in the design of the platform. Kim Sand Andersen has 24 employees
and 27 trucks under his command.
He takes pride in traﬃc safety in terms of
protecting his employees and further advances his trucks to assist the drivers during
their day by improving the conditions under which they work. During the one hour
interview with Andersen, his phone rang seven times emphasizing the busy nature
of being a hauler of a ﬂeet this size. Once again, a limited amount of requests to the
platform were found besides that Andersen would prefer to be able to do potential
sharing at his company computer where he does most of his work.
After the meetings with Ancker and Andersen, persona #1, #2, and #3 in Table
4.8 were created as presented in Appendix L.1. They would both welcome a video
platform and set few demands in that regard.

design phase can potentially result in a product not suited for the haulers, hence
the importance of personas in order to clasp a tangible idea for whom the platform
is developed. The full interviews can be found in Appendix I from which seven data
attributes were identiﬁed:
