4.3.2
Insurance Perspective
To understand the users from the insurance perspective, it was decided to go to
the source of the hypothesized business case at CODAN. As such, an interview
with Dorte Palmblad was arranged. Palmblad is a risk consultant and a proper
representation within this aspect. Due to logistics, it was not possible at the time
of development to engage with the second group of insurance users.
Therefore,
Palmblad substituted as a claims agent as well.
Going into this meeting, the authors had an idea of developing a system between
hauler and insurance company from which the AUGIT footage could be shared and
used in fault claims; A system that would be integrated with the current systems
43
==PAGE:56==
Master Thesis
4. Analysis
used in CODAN. This project expectation built upon written communication as
personal meetings were not an option in the ﬁrst period of the project as Palmblad
had previous arrangements. During the meeting, the project expectations quickly
changed as it was never the intended case to have an integrated platform developed.
The idea in itself was appreciated as it complements the workﬂow of the company
and could reduce the back and forth e-mails with the customers who could then
save time on both sides and potentially improve the customer experience as well.
However, the procedures of an insurance case are not straight forward. There are
no limitations on an insurance claim, and the system would need to store used and
unused videos for an undeﬁned period. She stresses that unused videos are a waste
of resources. Instead, Palmblad proposes a shared online platform between haulers
and insurance companies where the video can be accessed with limited eﬀort. As
such, multiple insurance companies and haulers can beneﬁt from the solution and
not just CODAN. Although a more ﬂexible solution than ﬁrst adopted, this solution
provided the opportunity to look further into the possibilities of the video in other
contexts as no time was needed to understand the current system at CODAN for
proper integration. In relation to other contexts, by developing an online platform,
Palmblad proposed to also provide the video as a research remedy by the cause of its
novelty to the industry. With this perspective, the footage could be more eﬀectively
adopted into aiding drivers through accident prevention.
As such, the meeting was concluded with a new perspective on how to realize the
desired insurance tool. In addition, this was the launch of the second perspective
of research to be included in the project and the formation of an online platform.

4.4
Product Design Analysis
The ﬁnal system needs a series of components to work collectively in order to fulﬁll
the requirements; An abstraction is presented in Figure 4.4.
Figure 4.4: Abstract product design
The product design is kept at an abstract level by the cause of multiple diﬀerent
possible solutions varying in the technical implementation. Additional investigation
is needed to establish a detailed design; however, for this project, the abstraction
is enough to express the requirements. In order for the hauler to have a minimal
workload with maintaining the data, this should be done automatically, i.e., the
video should be combined with the relevant data from the tachograph. In addition,
47
==PAGE:60==
Master Thesis
4. Analysis
the abstraction presents an automated upload to the platform whenever the truck
is within connection range. This was the solution hypothesized during the interview
with Andersen. Alternatively, it could be an interaction similar to that of retrieving
the tachograph data, which is done every twentieth day.
Figure 4.5: Recording interaction wireframe
While most system design is kept abstract, the recording interaction has been
provided with further details as the literature has shown promising results in such a
strategy. Based on the assumption that drivers are willing to be the data subject, the
product design is expanded with a wireframe to elaborate on the driver interaction.
This design reﬂects the notion of the opt-out policy with the intent of nudging
the drivers to produce more recordings but the option to use the glasses without
recording remains. Furthermore, the deliberate act of putting on the glasses for the
recording to start resolves the issue of being under the Danish TV Surveillance Law.
The aim of this project is not to develop the product but to validate the user
needs and further provide security guides for the implementation by cause of privacy
concerns. The former will be evaluated based on user interaction and perception of
the interface, i.e., the web application design. As such, in order to develop a suitable
prototype, these tests must be conceptualized so that the prototype can reﬂect the
desired aspects with respect to the requirements.
4.5
Test Design Analysis
In addition to validating the desire for the tool, a further issue presented itself as part
of the previous analysis, namely that the data subject could prevent the platform
from success by not providing the video. This is an aspect that should have been
validated prior to development, but as the issue came late in the process, this will
be evaluated in parallel with development which can burst or bear the design. As
such, two overall validations are required: The web application and the data subject.
48
==PAGE:61==
4. Analysis
DTU
4.5.1
Web Application Validation
The ﬁrst part is to validate the desire for the tool; therefore, technical aspects are
not considered for testing, only user perception, and interaction. This can, to some
extent, be derived through the interviews with the two user groups of haulers and
insurance agents, which were carried out with a positive notion during this analysis.
However, one thing is to ask straight forward if the platform would be appreciated;
another is to show the tool and then ask the same question. Does the interaction
with the platform present unknown barriers that were not thought of at the time
of ideation? As interaction is highly important for web-design in general, usability
tests are appropriate to carry out in order to observe user behavior and diﬃculties
while performing diﬀerent tasks.
To this aim, thinking aloud provides the desired insight into the users’ attitude
towards the system and will, therefore, be the chosen method. Often usability tests
are recorded for subsequent inspections in order to identify ’ﬂaws’ in the system.
This approach is expensive in terms of time, which, at this stage in the process, is a
resource that has to be carefully managed. Instead, the Instant Data Analysis (IDA)
approach by Kjeldskov et al.[76] is adopted for the usability evaluation. Furthermore,
’Easy’ was a clear requirement from both haulers. As such, the system’s ease of
learning is a desired feature to evaluate, for which the IDA will be combined with
questions often asked to accomplish a Cognitive Walkthrough:
• Does the user understand that a subtask is needed to accomplish a goal?
• Will the user notice that the action is available?
• Will the user understand that the subtask can be achieved through the action?
• Does the user get appropriate feedback?
In addition, to further investigate the ease of learning, the participant base will be
novel users who have not had previous interactions with the platform.
4.5.2
Data Subject Tests
The issue of validating that the data subject is not opposed the video recording is
conducted in two stages. The ﬁrst part is an experiment based on the observation
that participants who had knowledge about the system and tried the glasses were
not opposed the platform. The experiment will reﬂect a real-environment scenario,
i.e., the drivers will act as the data subject and then take a stand in regards to
whether or not they approve of the system.
Also, a second questionnaire will be developed based on learnings in Herning. It was
assumed that the questionnaire did not provide proper details for the participants to
answer the questions as intended. As such, a revised questionnaire will be created
based on the inexpensive nature and the advantage of being separated from the
’good-participant’ role.
49
==PAGE:62==
Master Thesis
4. Analysis

Web Application Design
For the ﬁrst prototype, the requirements presented as user stories, have been

work together: The interface and the database.
Although previously concluded out of scope, a simple database is desirable for ease
in development and testing. Therefore, a local database will be implemented for the

Table 4.12: Summary of data required by three diﬀerent instances being the hauler, the

50
==PAGE:63==
4. Analysis
DTU
The alert reader might notice that Law is now represented as an instance ’who
made requirements’ in Table 4.12. By deﬁnition, the law does not require that an
ID for people is present on the platform, however, it does require that people can
have their data removed. When regarding video, this can in some circumstances be
accomplished through facial recognition software but in this case where the face of
the driver will never occur on the video some other identiﬁer have to be present. If
not, the platform responsible would have to manually go through each video when
a person delete request was administered and try to ﬁnd every video of that said
person.
It is evident that a platform for videos is most beneﬁcial if videos are present. In
this case, it concerns two aspects: 1) that the videos are not deleted, and 2) that
the videos are recorded. To nudge the optimal realization of both some strategies,
based on the work of Caraban et al.[53], are found appropriate.
Facilitate With the intention of having the video on the platform, ’delete’ is
expressed through red colors and ’keep’ through green. Also, multiple options
are provided to keep a video.
Confront When deleting a video, the consequences should be displayed. Research
suggests two diﬀerent approaches based on gender.
Social Inﬂuence Leverage public commitment, i.e., have it so that other people
can see if the recording is started (spotlight eﬀect).
Fear Express that video is needed to avoid loss. Again, the message should be
gender-related. This aspect is more for marketing purposes.
Reinforce Just-in-time prompts before sharing to lessen mistakes
4.6.2
Data Recording
The second prototype is used to validate that the data subject is not opposed to
the platform. The underlying concern is that the data subject is oﬀended by the
invasion of privacy, along with being monitored. With the assumption that the
video, in combination with GPS tracking, reﬂects the worst possible scenario of
being monitored, this is an ideal starting point when it comes to ﬁnding the driver’s
limit.
Based on the current functionality of the AUGIT prototype glasses provided for this
project, three components need to work together in order to reﬂect the proposed
scenario.
Figure 4.7: Hardware components for additional prototype
51
==PAGE:64==
Master Thesis
4. Analysis
The required components are displayed in Figure 4.7. The glasses must be advanced
to support the recording of video and complimented with GNSS data from a phone
as this is likewise not currently available through the glasses. The last piece in the
puzzle is a device to provide a visual representation of what is being recorded while
driving for subsequent inspection used for validation. For this purpose, a laptop is
used.
4.7
Web Application
A web application framework consists of front-end and back-end technologies to
help the developer manage and build the desired application. AUGIT Solutions’
developers asked for a system written in the C# language using the .NET framework
for maintainability reasons. As this platform will be developed as a web application,
ASP.NET Core will be used, which is an extension to the regular .NET Core
framework, that ships with a base framework for processing web requests[77].
4.7.1
ASP.NET Core
ASP.NET Core is a server-side open-source, cross-platform framework created by
Microsoft that allows the developer to create web applications, web services, and
dynamic content-driven websites[78].
Furthermore, does ASP.NET Core built on the principle of middleware pipeline,
which is a set of instructions for how each HyperText Transfer Protocol (HTTP)
request should be handled in a given application.
HTTP is a stateless request-
response communication protocol formulated in plain text.
The order of which the middleware components used in an ASP.NET Core
application are added deﬁnes the exact order that the components are invoked on
request and the reversed order for the response[79]. Visual Studio can be used to
develop ASP.NET Core applications while also providing a server where the web
application can be executed and tested.
4.7.2
Web Server
The web application prototype will be developed in ASP.NET Core 2.2, and the
webserver to be used has to be free of charge by cause of project ﬁnances. ASP.NET
Core applications are run with an in-process HTTP server that listens for HTTP
requests and delivers them to the application as a HttpContext, which is an object
containing HTTP-speciﬁc information about a request. In this project, two HTTP
servers have been examined: Kestrel and Internet Information Services[80].
52
==PAGE:65==
4. Analysis
DTU
Kestrel
Kestrel is the default in-process cross-platform HTTP server for ASP.NET Core
developed by Microsoft. Kestrel can have two diﬀerent conﬁguration setups. It can
be used as the front end edge server, or it can have a reverse proxy in front of it to
receive the HTTP requests from the internet and forward them to the kestrel server
as seen in Figure 4.8.
Figure 4.8: Reverse proxy server in front of Kestrel
ASP.NET Core 2.1 or later applications support both hosting conﬁgurations.
However, having Kestrel as an edge server does not support sharing the same IP
address and port among multiple processes. This can be resolved by adding a reverse
proxy that allows for sharing the IP and port[81].
Internet Information Services
The Internet Information Services (IIS) is a web server that ships with ASP.NET
Core for Windows users. In order for IIS to handle requests between itself and an
HTTP server, the ASP.NET Core Module is used. The ASP.NET Core Module is
a native IIS module that can host the application inside of an IIS worker process,
which is known as in-process hosting, or it can forward all the HTTP web requests
to a Kestrel server located at the back-end which is running the application. This
is known as out-of-process hosting[82].
In-process hosting
The web application includes a server implementation known as the IIS HTTP server
when IIS is hosted with the in-process model. Figure 4.9 shows the in-process model
and illustrates how the diﬀerent components of the web server are communicating.
Figure 4.9: In-process hosting
The IIS receives an HTTP request from the internet, which it delivers to the
ASP.NET Core Module. The module passes it to the IIS HTTP Server that converts
the request from native to managed in order for the application’s logic to understand
it. When the request has been converted, it is passed on as a HttpContext to the
53
==PAGE:66==
Master Thesis
4. Analysis
application. After the request has been processed, a response is generated and send
back to the user in the reversed order the request was received.
Out-of-process hosting
Out-of-process hosting with IIS only works with Kestrel as the back-end HTTP
server. The ASP.NET Core module will manage the process of communicating with
the web application by having the application in a separate process. The module
will start the ASP.NET Core application when the ﬁrst request has been received,
and in case the application for some reason crashes or shuts down, the module will
restart it.
Figure 4.10: Out-of-process hosting
Figure 4.10 shows the relationship between the components of an out-of-process
model and how they are interacting. First of the IIS receives HTTP requests from
the internet and from here the module forwards the requests to the Kestrel server
on a port chosen at start-up. Several checks are performed in order to detect if the
requests came from the module. If not, the requests will be rejected. Furthermore,
the module does not support HTTPS forwarding to the HTTP server, even if the
original request to IIS was over HTTPS. When Kestrel gets a request, it is passed to
the application’s logic as a HttpContext object where a response will be established.
The response is passed back to the IIS, which sends it to the client that initiated
the request.
Web Server Conclusion
IIS in-process hosting provides improved performance over out-of-process hosting as
it avoids the extra network hop, furthermore does Microsoft also recommends this
approach when working with IIS[80]. Kestrel can be used as an edge server which
removes the extra network jump, but this setup does not support sharing the same
IP and port among multiple processes[81]. Due to that being a potential need in
future expansions of the platform as well as the increased performance gain, IIS
with in-process hosting has been selected.
4.8
Database
All relevant data regarding drivers and insurance companies should be kept safe, but
still, be accessible when needed. This data can be gathered from sources such as
tachographs and user inputs but require a place to be stored in order to manage the
data. Two diﬀerently database approaches: SQL and NoSQL have been examined
to determine which one should be used within this project.
54
==PAGE:67==
4. Analysis
DTU
4.8.1
SQL
A Structured Query Language (SQL) database consists of tables where each row is
a distinct entity, and each column is an attribute for that entity. Because of the
distinct structured relationships between rows and columns, the database can ensure
that atomicity, consistency, isolation, and durability are in place. Atomicity refers
to the integrity of the database’s transactions, where either a transaction completely
succeeds, or it is rolled back. In order for a SQL database to operate as intended, it
has to follow the conﬁgured data validation rules which enforce consistency. If data
in any other form is trying to be written to the database, the transaction will most
likely be rolled back. Isolation refers to the database being able to concurrently
process multiple transactions without one transaction aﬀects another, and acts as
they were being executed sequentially. To establish durability, every transaction will
be considered permanent once it has been committed to the database, even if failures
occur after the transaction. When a database posses these components, it is said to
be ACID compliant, which provides integrity of the data, eliminates the problem of
tables becoming "out-of-sync" and persistence in the event of failure[83, 84].
4.8.2
NoSQL
When databases do not require ACID compliance, or if the compliance would hinder
the database in any way, NoSQL can be used. NoSQL, in general, relies on the BASE
model that consists of three principles, namely, Basic Availability, Soft State, and
Eventual Consistency. Basic Availability means that the database will guarantee the
availability of data, and there will be a response to any request. The response could
be the data requested, a failure to obtain it, or the data could be in an inconsistent
state. The Soft State principle refers to the database being able to change state
over time, and ﬁnally, Eventual Consistency means that the database will at some
point become consistent when it stops getting inputs: All the data will propagate
to where it belongs. NoSQL databases are based on ﬂexibility and scalability and
are therefor good for rapid and ﬂexible development[85, 86, 84].
Conclusion
A structured database is chosen as this approach will provide the platform with
a more eﬃcient query execution and data retrieval. Furthermore are all data to
be stored in the database structured, which also points towards a SQL database.
Due to GDPR, a person has the right to ask for all information that can identify
him/her direct or indirect. If the database was based on a NoSQL approach, the
same data regarding the subject could be stored in several places such as a full
name. This would make it harder to delete everything about a subject as it could
55
==PAGE:68==
Master Thesis
4. Analysis
occur in multiple places. By using a standard SQL database, the name of a person
would only be stored in one place do the structure of the data.
The SQL database chosen for this project will be free of charge as it is still a
prototype and an Object-Relational-Mapper (ORM) will be used to abstract away
from the database. Several SQL databases ﬁll the criteria of the prototype such
as PostgreSQL, MySQL, Oracle XE, and Microsoft SQL server express.
As the
platform will be developed in ASP.NET Core 2.2, Microsoft SQL server express
with the Entity Framework Core will be the chosen database setup.
4.9
Security
The project platform will interact with users and ﬁles, locally and over the internet
to provide the service in need. To secure the platform in the best possible way,
several security measures will be discussed in order to determine the fundamental
security implementations needed. It is assumed that up-to-date browsers are used,
and therefore support modern web approaches.
4.9.1
Data Protection
The project platform will store pseudonym data about the drivers and their
associated trips. This data combined with the video data captured from a driver’s
perspective is sensitive data, and it is therefore of great importance to secure the
data and keep it out of reach of unauthorized people.
The video data could be required to be stored safely by the national government
of which the footage was captured and should, therefore, be compliant with their
standards.
One such example is the Federal Information Processing Standard
(FIPS), which is a set of approved standards that describe how data should be
processed in order to be compliant with the US government[87].
The FIPS publications are issued by the National Institute of Standards and
Technology (NIST). Publication 140-2 is a computer security standard that is used
to approve hardware and software components of a system[88]. As the concerned
system is merely a prototype, only the software algorithms will be FIPS compliant.
In order to secure the data at rest, it will be encrypted with a symmetric key
algorithm. Advanced Encryption Standard (AES) is a symmetric block cipher that
encrypts 128bits blocks of data with a key having a length of 128, 192 or 256
bits. The encryption scheme is FIPS-approved and was published as a standard
in November 2001[89]. AES works on a 128bit block and is therefore in need of a
mode that can feed the algorithm blocks of this size. Several block cipher encryption
modes for AES have been approved by the NIST where two have been selected to
elaborate further upon: Cipher Block Chaining (CBC) and Counter mode (CTR).
56
==PAGE:69==
4. Analysis
DTU
CBC works by dividing the plaintext to be encrypted into equal blocks, where each
of these blocks is xored with the previously created ciphertext block. The plaintext
P is turned into a sequence of blocks Pi for i = 1, ..., b, where b is the number of
blocks that the plaintext can be divided into. The standard formulation of CBC is
as follows: Ci = EK(Pi ⊕Ci−1) where EK is the encryption scheme with the key
K and Ci represents a ciphertext block for the i iteration. As the ﬁrst plaintext
block P1 does not have a previous ciphertext block available an initialization vector
(IV) is used. The IV is a randomly generated value, and should be sent with the
encrypted data as it is needed for decryption, hence C0 = IV . CBC requires that
the plaintext P is an exact multiple of the block size, which in this project is 128
bit. In order to ensure this property, the plaintext P will be padded.
A nonce is a contradiction of the words"number used once" and represents a value
that should never be reused in combination with the same key. The same goes for
the IV in CBC (which could be a nonce), as leakage of such information can create
vulnerabilities that can be exploited by an attacker and lead to retrieving knowledge
about the plaintext.
CTR is a stream cipher that uses a nonce of some form which is concatenated with a
counter value. This value is then encrypted with the key to forming a single block of
the keystream KI. The CTR mode is deﬁned as: Ki = EK(nonce||i) for i = 1, ..., b
such that the cipher block can be generated as Ci = Pi ⊕Ki. In order to decrypt
the message, the same approach is used except that Pi will be changed with Ci;
hence, the decryption will be Pi = Ci ⊕Ki. The counter and nonce have to ﬁt into
a single block, but as this project uses AES, the block size should be suﬃcient. The
shared block can be divided such that the nonce has the ﬁrst 96 bits (12 bytes) and
the 32 remaining bits (4 bytes) are for the counter i. This will limit the system to
encrypting 296 times if all values were used before the key had to change in order to
avoid repeatable pairs of nonce and key. Furthermore will the max size for a ﬁle that
has to be encrypted be (232−1)∗128
8
bytes which are ≈68.71 Gigabytes. This message
size should be suﬃcient to encrypt video ﬁles generated by the AR glasses[90].
Authentication and Authorization
As the data should not be freely accessible, a user has to log into the system by
providing the web application with a username and password. The password will
be matched with the one in the database and if it succeeds the user is authenticated
and a cookie is delivered to the users browser. All passwords in the system will be
salted and hashed with a FIPS-approved hashing algorithm such as Password Based
Key Derivation Function 2 (PBKDF2)[91] with a high number of iterations in order
to slow it down to increase security against brute force attacks. An account on the
web application will have an attribute associated with it which determines the role
of a user. The role will reﬂect what kind of rights the user has access to and to what
57
==PAGE:70==
Master Thesis
4. Analysis
extent the user can manipulate data within the system.
4.9.2
Web Session
A web session consists of information exchange between a browser and a server,
which starts with the browser sending an HTTP request for a resource located on a
server, and the server answering with a response. HTTP traﬃc can be intercepted
and read by anyone listening on the network.
This makes all data transmitted
over an HTTP connection unprotected and unable to guarantee several security
properties. The data exchange can be protected by using the HTTPS protocol,
which creates an encrypted channel between two endpoints using Transport Layer
Security (TLS) or Secure Sockets Layer (SSL)[92, 93].
Session Data
In order for a user to sign in once (for a time period), instead of signing in on
every server request, information regarding the session has to be stored. This data
can either be stored at the browser side or the server-side. Storing the information
at the server side is known as a stateful session, where the server may generate a
unique identiﬁer for that speciﬁc web session, and send it to the browser. When
the browser provides the unique identiﬁer to the server, the server will look up the
identiﬁer and return the session data for that session. Storing the information at
the browser side is known as a stateless session. A stateless session does not rely on
the server to capture and hold the data; instead, the browser itself is responsible. In
order to prevent tampering of the data, the server can use cryptography to protect
the information and do checks on the identiﬁer whenever it is received[93].
Cookies and Web Storage
Cookies in an abstract view is a key-value pair that is generated by a web server to
be sent to a browser where it will be stored. Whenever the browser with the stored
cookie sends a request to the server, the cookie will automatically be attached to
the request. When the server receives the request with the given cookie, the cookie
is inspected, and if valid, the session will be resumed[93, 94].
Web storage is a feature of HTML5 that works as a storage unit on the browser
side. Opposite cookies that are attached to every request, web storage data stays in
the browser unless it is manually attached to the HTTP(S) request. Modiﬁcations
to the behavior can be made if desired. Web storage oﬀers two ways of storing the
data at the browser; the ﬁrst is local storage which persists until explicitly deleted
and the second is session storage that only holds the information until the session
is terminated[93].
58
==PAGE:71==
4. Analysis
DTU
Same-Origin Policy
The Same-Origin Policy (SOP) is a security policy that acts as a defense for browsers
to prevent theft of sensitive credentials by scripts of untrusted websites. The policy
enforces a strict separation between unrelated sites such that credentials owned
by one site cannot be accessed by another site unless they share the same origin.
An origin is a combination of a scheme, a hostname and a port number such as
https,Codata.com,443[93, 92]. Web storage will only give a script running on-site
access to an origin if it runs in the exact same origin itself. The cookie jar containing
the cookies works in the same way, but the deﬁnition of an origin regarding cookies
are more relaxed as it is not based on scheme nor port[93].
Using web storage to store session credentials, the platform will have to implement
enough logic to be able to attach them to an HTTP(S) request, whereas cookies will
automatically attach the credentials with every request.
Cookies provide better
conﬁdentiality than the web storage by default, as they can be protected with
predeﬁned attributes.
4.9.3
Cookie Session Hijacking
When a user authenticates himself to a server, a cookie is sent to the user’s
browser and acts as the only proof of the user’s identity for that session. A server
will not be able to distinguish two users from each other if they have the same
cookie, which leads to problems regarding the hijacking of session cookies. If an
attacker obtains a cookie that contains the identiﬁcation of a user, the cookie
can be set into the attacker’s browser who can then impersonate the original
user.
To prevent this leakage, it is essential to ensure the conﬁdentiality of the
session cookies by preventing the cookie from being shared to untrusted related
domains[95]. Furthermore, cookies using the attribute HttpOnly will be limited to
HTTP(S) requests and therefor unavailable to malicious JavaScripts injected into
trusted sites[92]. Cookies with the attribute Secure are never sent through HTTP
requests but only over HTTPS so that the cookie will never be exposed to an
attacker listening to the HTTP traﬃc[95]. Also, fresh session cookies should always
be provided upon log-in to prevent session fixation[96].
Another exploitable
vulnerability a developer has to be aware of is the domain cookie attribute. An
attacker hosting a malicious website on a sibling domain of the target website can
set cookies with the domain attribute which will be sent to the target website. The
cookie will be indistinguishable from all of the target website’s directly set cookies.
The attacker can now force the browser to access the target website via HTTP and
set cookies to be sent to the target website over HTTPS. These sorts of attacks can
be used to force a user’s browser to use the attacker’s session. To prevent this attack,
a browser must not be able to open an HTTP connection to a website. Setting a
website to redirect HTTP to HTTPS is not enough as the connection still happens.
59
==PAGE:72==
Master Thesis
4. Analysis
A web application can enforce HTTP Strict Transport Security(HSTS) which will
block all HTTP requests[92, 93].
4.9.4
Cross-Site Request Forgery
Cross-Site Request Forgery (CSRF) attacks are built on parsing an HTTP(S) request
through a user’s browser to a website where the user already has an authenticated
session, hence a valid cookie for that session. The attack could have a malicious
website that contains HTML elements that are pointing to the website the user has
a valid cookie at. When one of these elements is being executed, a request will be
sent to the website from the user’s browser where the authentication cookie for the
user will automatically be attached to the request[92, 97].
Several solutions exist to prevent CSRF attacks, whereas the most eﬃcient solution
to prevent the attacks against web applications is bypassing a pseudo-random token
through a hidden ﬁeld in the trusted HTML element[98]. When the application
receives the request, it can validate the token in the session with the token in the
request, where a match indicates that the request came from the same origin[98].
This solution is known as the Synchronizer Token Pattern (STP) and relies on web
applications to implement it in order to protect a user.
This project will not look into the prevention of CSRF attacks from the user side, but
solutions exist that can protect a user without relying on web applications such as
CookieArmor[97]. CookieArmor is a client-side internet proxy that scans incoming
and outgoing packets from the client’s machine to protect the user without the need
for STP implemented on web applications visited.
4.9.5
SQL Injection Attacks
SQL Injection Attacks (SQLIA) are threats against database systems where an
attacker manages to inject string inputs that change a SQL statement executed
by the application. This attack can lead to a data breach where the attacker can
retrieve, manipulate, and delete sensitive data stored in a database[99]. OWASP’s
top 10[100] web attacks and Swascan’s top 5[101] web attacks, both rank SQLIA high
on the list. The project platform will be using SQL databases to store most data,
and should, therefore, be protected in the best possible manner against these forms
of attacks. SQLIAs rely on the ability to modify or append SQL statements to the
original desired query. Using an ORM to generate the SQL statements and always
use parameterization for SQL queries will protect the application against most
SQLIAs. Queries created with string concatenation or interpolation and executed
by the ORM can still be attacked since the original string can be manipulated.
Therefore, it is important for the developers to provide safe and secure queries for
the ORM to execute[102, 103, 104].
60
==PAGE:73==
4. Analysis
DTU
4.9.6
Looking Towards The Future
Quantum computing has been giving a substantial amount of research, and if
suﬃciently advanced quantum computers are developed, they can become a threat
to the current key-based encryption schemes used worldwide[105]. Unlike standard
computers having one or zero in a state, quantum computers can handle both
one and zero simultaneously, which is called the superposition quantum state.
Quantum computers use these superposition states and quantum entanglements to
perform simultaneous calculations and thereby outperform a normal computer[106].
Quantum computing has not been a focus area for this project; hence, a deep
understanding of this matter has not been established. The research into quantum
computing has only served as a step to try and understand the consequences that
it can bring.
In theory, quantum computers should be able to solve the mathematical problems
that public key encryption algorithms are built upon; hence, these encryption
schemes can become insecure[105]. Brute-force attacks against symmetric generated
ciphers are sped up by roughly a quadratic factor, which means that the size of the
key used to generate the ciphers should roughly be doubled to withstand such an
attack.
TLS and SSL use public-key encryption to exchange symmetric keys between two
endpoints. This approach, in a post-quantum era, is insecure, and an attacker can
decrypt all data transmitted on such a channel. In order to secure symmetric key
encryption, the key size should be 256 bits as this is the quadratic factor of a 128-bit
key which is considered safe[107]. AES will, therefore, be using the highest available
key for the scheme, 256 bit.
61
==PAGE:74==
==PAGE:75==

65
==PAGE:78==
Master Thesis
5. Design
Figure 5.5 illustrates the data ﬂow for the host server. The web server hosts the
web application, which uses the Model-View-Controller (MVC) architecture design
pattern.
The MVC pattern separates the application into three interconnected
segments, namely, model, view, and controller. The model is in charge of managing
data in the application and does not rely on any of the other segments. The views
are using the razor view engine to coat .NET code into HTML markup and are
responsible for displaying the graphical content in association with data retrieved
from the model. The controllers are handling user interactions and are responsible
for ensuring that the correct model object is chosen and parsed to the view where the
view is rendered. Furthermore, are the controllers also responsible for responding
to the user interactions[108].
The database server, seen to the left in Figure 5.5 stores all data for the web
application, except for the video ﬁles. The video ﬁles are stored in the ﬁle system
and the path to them are stored in the database. The web application can query
the database and retrieve relevant information to display for the clients. When the
platform retrieves a set of HGV data from the HGV server presented in Figure 5.4,
the web application will extract all information from the data and store it in the
database except for the video. This will be encrypted and stored on the ﬁle system.

5.4
Development Iterations
The development of the platform was separated into four sprints to support
agile development. Each sprint focus area is described below rather than in the
Implementation chapter, as this will depict the ﬁnal implementation.
5.4.1
Sprint 0
The initial sprint was concerned with the general setup of the platform.
This
included the MVC architecture pattern, the Integrated Development Environment
(IDE) followed by the SQL database tables containing the attributes that were found
needed from all perspectives, i.e., Hauler, Insurance, and Research as it was not
68
==PAGE:81==
5. Design
DTU
known if all were to be fully implemented. The web application on the host server
was created with the ASP.NET Core 2.2 framework, which was then connected to
the database through Entity Framework Core.
5.4.2
Sprint 1
The aim of sprint 1 was to implement the hauler functionality from the presented
MVP. During this sprint, several issues with the setup of the database were
encountered, and it had to be reﬁned several times with minor changes. The ﬁrst
and second iteration was related to the Index page (ﬁrst page) and what data
should be accessible without further action.
The third iteration concerned the
Details page and further iterations about the insurance sharing mechanism. During
development, heuristics were carried out by the authors, and usability was tested
with students from DTU and user substitutes. As the recruitment of haulers proved
to be harder than expected, students were used to ﬁnd mistakes in the design and
implementation before ﬁnal validation.
The ﬁnal stage in validating the hauler
perspective regrettably did not happen.
5.4.3
Sprint 2
The aim of sprint 2 was to implement the insurance functionality from the presented
MVP. The process was similar to that of the former sprint, however, with fewer
database issues. In a similar fashion heuristics and initial usability was conducted
with tech students at DTU, but the ﬁnal validation was carried out with the target
group being claims agents.
5.4.4
Sprint 3
The ﬁnal sprint was originally intended for security measures. However, with the
additional test being hypothesized, this sprint was dedicated to advance the AR
glasses to support video recording. As a consequence, the security measures were
implemented in parallel with the diﬀerent views. To ensure data conﬁdentiality,
the video ﬁle has been encrypted at rest. Furthermore, are all data in transit also
encrypted with a key shared between the HGV server and the host server. User
interactions and web sessions with the application have also been looked into in
order to secure the platform against some known web-based attacks.
69
==PAGE:82==
==PAGE:83==
Implementation 6
The prototype is developed using multiple hardware and software components. The
hardware components are the DreamWorld DreamGlass, the OnePlus 3 mobile
phone, and a laptop. The glasses are connected to the laptop through two USB
cables and an HDMI. The mobile is likewise connected to the laptop through a
USB. The laptop acts as both the host server and the HGV server and will run all
code associated with the prototype. This chapter presents selected segments of the
ﬁnal prototype implementation and describes how they work together in order to
acquire and share data.
6.1
HGV Server Side
The HGV server is executing a Unity program that is responsible for logging the
GNSS data, starting the recording of video footage, converting the video footage
and encrypting all the data. The mobile application Share GPS is used to collect
the location data of the mobile device.
6.1.1
Tachograph Data
No real tachograph data was gathered; hence, the tachograph data used in this
project has been created as a text ﬁle holding information that is available through
a digital tachograph[25]. This data is stored as a text ﬁle and is completely readable
by the human eye. Appendix O.2 shows an example of how the data is formatted.
6.1.2
GNSS Data
The laptop does not have a GNSS tracking device incorporated into its design, so the
mobile phone will be used to simulate this attachment. In order to transfer the GNSS
data from the mobile phone to the laptop, the tool Android Debug Bridge (ADB)
is used. ADB is a client/server command-line tool for computers to communicate
with devices running Android.
Share GPS
Share GPS is a free of charge application available for Android devices and is used
to share GNSS data with mapping applications such as Google Earth[109]. The
71
==PAGE:84==
Master Thesis
6. Implementation
application can share data in various formats, including the National Marine Elec-
tronics Association(NMEA) GNSS format which is used in this prototype. NMEA
GNSS data consist of diﬀerent sentences that each has a tag representing the form
of data captured[110]. Appendix O.1 shows examples of NMEA sentences send from
the mobile device and received by the laptop. All sentences have a tag associated,
which consists of a talker ID and a message description. For the tag GNRMC, the
talker ID is GN, indicating that the data from this sentence is based on the Global
Navigation Satellite System(GNSS). The last part of the tag RMC stands for the
recommended minimum data for GNSS and indicates the format of the data in the
sentence, and how it is divided. Consider the full sentence:
GNRMC,094251.00,A,5600.854422,N,01228.519560,E,0.0„050619,0.7,E,A*0B
To provide an overview, Table 6.1 separates the sentence and describes each of the
respective ﬁelds.
Values
Name
Description
094251.00
Time-stamp in UTC+0
format: hhmmss.sss
A
Status of sentence
A=Active V=Navigation Receiver Warning
5600.854422
Latitude
format: dddmm.mmmm with leading zeros
N
Latitude Indicator
N=North S=South
01228.519560
Longitude
format: dddmm.mmmm with leading zeros
E
Longitude Indicator
E=East W=West
0.0
Speed over ground
Speed is measured in knots
Course over ground
Measured in degrees
050619
UTC Date
format: ddmmyy
0.7
Magnetic Variation
Measured in degrees
E
Magnetic Variation Indicator
E=East W=West
A*0B
Check-sum
Check-sum of sentence

6.1.4
HGV Data Encryption
Whenever a piece of data is complete it is encrypted in the Unity program by
a new thread and the original ﬁle is deleted. When a ﬁle has been encrypted a
tag representing the ﬁle type and an ID used to associate a video with a GNSS
log, are appended to the ﬁle. The FIPS-approved encryption library developed by
Bouncy Castle[112] AES scheme with the modes Cipher Block Chaining (CBC) and
Counter mode (CTR) has been used to encrypt ﬁles in the project. Both modes are
implemented, but the default one used is CTR. Listing 6.6 shows the encryption
part of the method AES_CTR_ENC used to encrypt generated ﬁles.
Listing 6.6: Encryption of ﬁle using Bouncy Castle
1 private
void
AES_CTR_ENC(string source , byte tag , string id =
null)
2 {
3
//key and IV set
4
MemoryOutputStream
bOut = new
MemoryOutputStream ();
5
ICipherBuilder <IParameters <Algorithm >> encryptorBldr =
6
provider. CreateEncryptorBuilder (FipsAes.Ctr.WithIV(iv));
7
ICipher
encryptor = encryptorBldr.BuildCipher(bOut);
8
using (Stream
encOut = encryptor.Stream)
9
{
10
encOut.Write(input , 0, input.Length);
11
}
12
byte [] cipherText = bOut.ToArray ();
13
...
14
File.WriteAllBytes(destination , cipherText);
15
File.Delete(source);
16 }
In line 4, the MemoryStream bOut, which will be used to hold the encrypted data, is
created. Line 5 creates a builder that takes the parameters FipsAes.Ctr.WithIV(iv),
indicating that the chosen encryption scheme is the FIPS-approved AES algorithm
with the CTR mode using the IV which were set earlier. The builder and the Mem-
oryStream are then used in combination to start an encryption stream. Line 10
shows how the write method writes a block of bytes to the current stream using
the data read from the input. The outcome is stored in the bOut stream, which is
written to a destination seen at line 14. After the newly created encrypted ﬁle has
been stored, the original ﬁle is deleted as a ﬁnal step.

6.2.1
Security
Listing 6.9 shows how the cookie attributes and HSTS are set in the startup class of
the web application. The listing includes two methods, namely ConfigureServices
seen at line 1 and Configure at line 13. The ConfigureServices is where services
such as cookie attributes can be added, whereas the Configure method act as
the middleware. Whenever a request is made to the web application, it will be
through the Configure method in the order top to bottom. It is therefore of great
importance that the pipeline’s conﬁgurations are in the correct order.

The database used for storing user data is a local database created by Visual Studio.
The database is not considered safe as it is stored in an unprotected ﬁle accessible
by the server and should only be used when developing prototypes. ASP.NET Core
identity is an authentication system that adds log-in and role-based functionality
to the project platform.
The identity service can be modiﬁed to store more or
less information, require two-factor authentication or anything else needed by the
project platform. The roles associated with a user is used to determine if the user is
allowed access to a page or action, and used to determine what information should
be displayed on a page.
A user’s password is salted and hashed using PBKDF2 by the ASP.NET Core 2.2
predeﬁned password hasher. The default iteration count set in ASP.NET Core 2.2
is 10000 iterations, which has been modiﬁed to 100000 iterations in the project
platform as presented in Listing 6.11.
Listing 6.11: ConﬁgureServices method in Startup.cs
1 services.Configure <PasswordHasherOptions >( options => options.
IterationCount = 100000);
To avoid SQLIA, all database calls do not use strings that can be manipulated.
Instead, the Entity Framework Core is used with Language Integrated Query
(LINQ). Listing 6.12 shows a simple LINQ executed in the project platform.

The keys used for encryption and decryption should be stored in a secure place.
In this project, keys are being stored in a separate location from the project tree
but are still associated with the project. The secret keys are stored in a ﬁle called
secrets.json. The ﬁle is not encrypted and fully accessible by anyone on the same
system. The keys should only be stored in this ﬁle under development and should
be moved to a secure place before the web application is launched. Listing 6.13
shows how the secrets.json ﬁle looks.
Listing 6.13: secrets.json ﬁle
1
" OneHaulierKey": " ThisIsTheSymmetricKeyInUse123456 ",
2
"ServerKey":
" ThisIsTheSymmetricKeyForServer12 "
The ﬁrst part in Listing 6.13, namely "OneHaulierKey" is the name of the key
whereas the second part "ThisIsTheSymmetricKeyInUse123456" is the actual key,
which in both cases are 32 bytes long. When the web application receives data from
the HGV server, it is decrypted, read, stored, and discarded, except for the video.
After the video has been decrypted with the haulier key, it is encrypted with the
server’s key seen in line 2 at Listing 6.13.
Security Headers
The site https://securityheaders.com/ analyze the security headers of a given
site and scores it based on the headers set.
As the project platform is not
deployed, NGROK[113] is used to route traﬃc from a public cloud service to the
NGROK process running on the machine and then to the local address of the web
application. When executing the command ngrok http -host-header=localhost
51814 NGROK start routing traﬃc. The last part of the command 51814 is the
port to the HTTP version of the web application as NGROK will be applying the
TLS for the website. Figure 6.2 shows how NGROK forwards the traﬃc and what
public address the web application is available at.
Figure 6.2: NGROK with "ngrok http -host-header=localhost 51814" command executed
80
==PAGE:93==
6. Implementation
DTU
Providing the https://securityheaders.com/ website with the NGROK url to the
web application, the security headers of the side can be analyzed. Based on the test
the following headers were added:
• Referrer-Policy
• Content-Security-Policy
• X-XSS-Protection
• X-Content-Type-Options
The referrer header contains information on where an inbound visitor came from and
can be instructed to deliver less to none information. The Content Security Policy
(CSP) deﬁnes what content has been approved to be loaded by the browser. The
browser will not load all content that has not been whitelisted. This policy can help
protect against Cross-Site Scripting (XXS) as scripts injected into a trusted website
are not whitelisted and therefore not loaded. The X-XSS-Protection response header
stops the page from loading to some degree speciﬁed if a cross-site scripting attack
is detected.
This security header is not necessary if a well-implemented CSP is
set unless the target browser does not support CSP. A Multipurpose Internet Mail
Extensions (MIME) consist of type/subtype and indicate a ﬁle’s nature. An image
could for example have the MIME image/png. If a MIME type is not deﬁned or the
browser believes it is incorrect, the browser can perform an action known as MIME
sniﬃng. This action is performed diﬀerently depending on the browser, and the
end goal of the action is to guess the correct MIME type. X-Content-Type-Options
ensures that the browser does not try to change or guess the MINE.
Listing 6.14: Implementation of security headers
1 public
void

IHostingEnvironment
env)

6. Implementation
Listing 6.14 shows the implementation of the four headers into the pipeline. Line
6 sets the referrer header to "no-referrer" which instructs the browser to omit
this header with requests that are made from the web application.
Lines 8-12
set the content security police, where line 11 approves a script with the nonce
DetailsHaulierRandomNonce. X-XSS-Protection is set to EnabledWithBlockMode
as seen in line 15 and prevents the browser from rendering the site if an attack on
the system is detected. The last header x content type options has one valid value
"nosniﬀ", which is set at line 16.
6.3
Limitations
As of now, the encrypted data created by the HGV server has to be manually
uploaded through the web application. For the platform to recognize and store the
data correctly, the uploaded data must consist of one ﬁle containing the tachograph
data followed by an arbitrary number of video and GNSS ﬁles.
Recording a video ﬁle using the DreamGlass SDK will save the ﬁle in .avi format.
The video is therefor converted, but the conversion is time-consuming and will scale
with the video size. For larger ﬁles in higher quality, this is an issue that has to be
addressed before the release of the platform.
When a user watches a video, the video is decrypted into a root folder in the project
from where it is displayed to the user.
As a consequence, the video is entirely
unprotected, and a solution to this problem has to be implemented before system
release. One solution could be to load it all into memory only, from where it could
be streamed to the user.
Undocumented behavior occurred while using the DreamGlass SDK. When the
quality of the image is adjusted to record and store in 720p, the playback speed of
the video was doubled, whereas the lowest quality 360p is played back slightly slower
than recorded. Furthermore, does the high-quality setting decrease the performance
of the AR elements drastically when enabled on the laptop.
The decrease in
performance happens with or without the script created to record, convert, encrypt,
and store the ﬁles.
82
==PAGE:95==
Tests and Results 7
During the course of the project, several tests and experiments have been conducted,
some of which are presented in this chapter. Concerning usability tests, this chapter
presents only the ﬁnal tests conducted, meanings that usability tests performed
with user substitutes and technical students in order to resolve immediate usability
problems are not presented. Instead, the list of problems identiﬁed during these
sessions can be found in Appendix P. Unfortunately, it was not possible to recruit
participants from the hauler perspective within the time frame of testing. As a
consequence, hauler usability tests remain to be completed.
7.1
Insurance Usability Test
The ﬁnal usability test, i.e., the usability conducted with insurance agents in
combination with validating the desire for the system was performed at the claims
department in Aarhus on May 29th. Prior arrangements were made for CODAN
to ’supply’ six claims agents.
At the time of arrival, four agents were ready to
participate in the usability test.
7.1.1
Setup
The laptop with the web application prototype was placed on a desk. An additional
laptop was placed to the left containing a powerpoint presentation with the tasks to
fulﬁll. This was done in order for the participants to have the same conditions, that
is, the questions were not altered in any way between participants, and they could
reread them if not understood the ﬁrst time. Behind the desk and the participant,
one examiner took notes while the other acted to engage the participant in thinking
aloud.
7.1.2
Participants
All participants work as claims agents at CODAN insurance company where they
handle claims for corporate clients. The recruitment was organized through the
manager of the department, Joan Brøgger Riis. Among the participants were two
males and two females between the ages of 26 to 51 (mean=34, std=9).
83

As can be observed in Table 7.2, no problems were categorized as critical. All tasks

of the participants. The problems are summarized as follows:
1 Participants would try to enter the site of ’Research’ when asked to search
for a case. This was a design ﬂaw in the prototype. The ﬁnal system should
not have the possibility for insurance agents to access another user’s page.
However, it was implemented in the prototype for developers to move from
one user page to another quickly. In addition, it could also be a language
barrier that was the cause of the problem as the prototype was developed in
English, and the participants were Danish. This was evident as one participant
read ’Research’ as ’Search’.
2 Participants did not immediately connect the phrase ’Close case’ on the web-
page with the task that was formulated in Danish. However, all participants
found the connection without further aid.
3 Participants did not use the search bar to ﬁnd insurance cases. All but one
noticed the search bar where only one sought to use it. This was caused by
the fact that the list only included ten cases, so the participants deduced that
it would take longer to enter the case number for the search.
4 Log-in is commented to be small but placed where the participant expected it
to be: "everyone with a normal computer knowledge would easily ﬁnd it"
5 As already hypothesized as a cause behind two of the found usability problems,
the language is considered a problem in general on the limit to Critical. This
a criticized design ﬂaw as it does not reﬂect the user.
6 Users had to redirect to the front-page every time they had to go back to the
list by cause of a missing ’back’ button

7.2
Driver Accept Experiment
Close to being halfway in the project, when analyzing the questionnaire answers from
the transport exhibition in Herning, a concern about the data subject approval came
to be. If the ﬁnalized product was to have a chance for success, it is depended on
this approval. As such, in order for the authors of this thesis to provide a satisfying
recommendation for AUGIT, the concern has to be put to rest. As such, the overall
objective of this experiment is to validate that:
Employees are willing to be the data subject in video for the employer, the
insurance company and traﬃc research respectively.
In addition, the initial contradictions in Herning were hypothesized to be caused
by a varied amount of information and especially the lack of information for
the participants to acquire a similar mental model of the video.
Therefore, the
experiment is designed so that it also can provide a suggestion as to whether prior
exposure to the video has an impact on user acceptance. If this is the case, the
ﬁnding can potentially contribute to later sales or marketing strategies for AUGIT.
The experiment is designed based on learnings and observations made in Herning.
As a result, the experiment excludes the use of interviews and will make sure
that all participants are introduced to the video to form a proper mental model
of the solution. The experiment is conducted as, and the order of participants is
randomized based on availability.
7.2.1
Setup
The participants are placed behind the wheel in a vehicle of their own choosing. A
Ford Ka is provided for the experiment. During the experiment, the participants
will wear AR glasses while driving. On the passenger seat is the examiner with the
additional equipment (laptop and mobile). The experiment is conducted on small
roads for safety measures.
Dependant
Independent
Confounding
User answer
Introduction
Quality
Table 7.4: Variables considered in the experiment

Table 7.4 presents the identiﬁed variables. The dependent variable used to answer
the main objective is an answer to the question: Which of the following would you
share the video with?
• Employer
• Insurance company
• Traﬃc researcher
• Neither of the above
The answers are given anonymously on paper to avoid the previously suspected
inﬂuence of interviews in the form of the good-participant role. The independent
variable is the introduction given to the video in the levels of no introduction and
visual introduction. The confounding variable of quality has also been identiﬁed.
The quality presented is low caused by unknown behavior of the SDK.

where driving was considered as part of their work. Among the ﬁnal participant

7.3
Driver Accept Questionnaire
Independent of the previous experiment, a questionnaire was shared once again
using social media. The questionnaire was created to undermine the concern of
the low number of participants recruited for the former experiment. It was active
for one week, from May 27th to June 3rd where 71 participants provided their
answers anonymously to 11 questions divided into two sections. Section 1 included
seven questions regarding personal history, such as gender, age, etc. and Section 2
included four questions regarding willingness to share the video from driving. The
questionnaire can be found in Appendix S.
7.3.1

The second part to validating drivers as data subjects was the questionnaire where 71
subjects answered in the running week of late May. As the questionnaire was shared
using social media, some participants inevitably fall too far from being a somewhat
fair representation of truck drivers. Table 7.8 represents the sorting criteria:
